The Mutakallimun's Proof that God Knows All Things
States the mutakallimun's argument that God knows all things, based on three premises to be established.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the mutakallimun's proof for God's comprehensive knowledge and the role of the three premises.

Core Idea
God's essence necessitates knowledge of all things, given the possibility.

The mutakallimun (theologians) advanced a concise argument to show that God knows everything. This lesson presents their reasoning in summary form, which rests on three key premises to be established. Understanding this argument sets the stage for the detailed proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'al-mutakallimun', 'al-ma'lumat', 'al-mujib', 'al-sihha', 'al-wujud'.
The argument is stated very concisely; don't worry if it seems brief.
2
Identify the three logical components
The passage contains three elements: (1) the validity (sihha) of God knowing each infinite thing, (2) the cause (mujib) being His specific essence, (3) the necessity (wajaba) that results when validity and cause combine. Note that the author says these are three premises to be established.
The three premises are not fully explained here; they will be expanded later.
3
Understand the argument's flow
Recognize the logical flow: If it is possible for God to know each thing, and the reason for His knowledge is His essence (which is constant), then He must know all things. This is a deductive argument that requires the premises to be proven.
Without the premises, the argument is incomplete. The next lessons will establish them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة : في تقرير دلالة المتكلمين في كونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات . قالوا : إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها، والموجب لكونه تعالى عالماً هو ذاته المخصوصة، ومتى كانت الصحة حاصلة في الكل، وكان الموجب هو ذاته المخصوصة وجب أن يكون عالماً بجميع المعلومات . وهذه مقدمات ثلاثة لا بد من تقريرها.
The third issue: Concerning the demonstration of the mutakallimun that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. They said: It is valid that He, exalted, be knowing of each one of the infinite known things, and the cause for His being knowing is His specific essence. And when validity is present in all, and the cause is His specific essence, then He must be knowing of all known things. And these are three premises that must be established.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage opens the third issue (mas'ala) and presents the argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) for God's comprehensive knowledge. The argument is stated in summary form: It is possible (ṣaḥḥa) for God to know each of the infinite known things. The cause (mūjib) for His being knowing is His specific essence (dhātuhu al-mukhṣṣaṣa). Since the possibility holds for all things and the cause is the same essence, He necessarily (wajaba) knows all things. The author then notes that this argument requires the establishment of three premises (muqaddimāt thalāth), which will be elaborated in the following text. The passage thus sets out the conclusion and the logical structure, leaving the detailed premises for subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
المتكلمون
المعلومات
الموجب
الصحة
الوجوب
المقدمات الثلاث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is complete here, but it only states the conclusion; the three premises are necessary to prove the validity and necessity.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'these are three premises that must be established.' The argument is a skeletal outline, not a full proof.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun's proof as stated, what is the cause (al-mūjib) for God's being knowing?
A) His will.
B) His specific essence.
C) The infinite known things.
D) His power.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's proof relies on the combination of possibility and the specific essence to conclude that God knows all things, but the proof requires establishing three premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your every sin is not caused by the sin itself but by His own essence, does that make His awareness more intimate or more terrifying? How does that change the way you approach repentance?
?
The mutakallimun argue that God's essence necessitates His knowledge of all things. Does your daily life reflect a deep conviction that God knows you completely at every moment, or do you sometimes act as if He overlooks your inner thoughts? What would change if you truly lived knowing His knowledge is as certain as His essence?
?
Since the same essence that necessitates God's knowledge also necessitates His justice, how does this affect your accountability for your hidden deeds? Will you today confess something to Him that you usually ignore, knowing His knowledge is inseparable from His essence?

First Objection: Are the Terms Synonymous or Different?
First objection: dilemma between synonymy and difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the claim that the three terms do not imply multiplicity, specifically the argument that they are either synonymous or different, leading to an invalidation of the original claim.

Core Idea
The first objection forces a choice: either the terms are redundant or they indicate multiplicity.

This lesson examines the first of three objections raised against the view that the terms ‘aql, ‘aqil, and ma‘qul carry a single meaning. The objector begins by asserting that the three terms must be either synonymous or different, and then shows that neither option supports the original claim. We will follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the first objection in Arabic and its translation to grasp the argument.
Focus on the logical disjunction: synonymous or different.
2
Analyze the two possibilities
Consider what follows if the terms are synonymous (redundancy) or different (must be negative or positive).
3
Understand why negative concepts are ruled out
The objector claims consciousness is positive, so negative is invalid.
This depends on an earlier proof not included here.
4
Conclude the implication
The terms are positive attributes of the essence, thus distinct and imply multiplicity.
This refutes the original claim.
5
Review the checkpoint
Answer the MCQ to ensure comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد (۱) ولقائل يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه : الأول : إن لفظ العقل والعاقل والمعقول إما أن تكون ألفاظاً مترادفة أو متباينة . فإن كان الأول لم يكن في ذكرها فائدة معنوية ، وإن كان الثاني فهي إما أن تكون مفهومات سلبية أو ثبوتية. والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك ليس مفهوماً سلبياً ، فبقي الثاني ، ولا شك أنها ليست مفهومات متباينة (۲) بل هي صفات لهذه الذات (۳) ، وحينئذ يبطل قوله : إن هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة لا تفيد كثرة في المعنى . الوجه الثاني : وهو أنا قد دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك معنى نسبي إضافي، فيمتنع كونه عين تلك الذات المخصوصة . الوجه الثالث : إن العقل يتفرع على حصول التعقل ، وكونه معقولاً لنفسه ، يتفرع على كونه عاقلاً لنفسه ، فهذه المفهومات الثلاثة يتفرع بعضها على البعض ، فيمتنع كونها مفهوماً واحداً . والله أعلم.
One (1) and a speaker says: The objection to it is from aspects: First: The terms 'aql, 'aqil, and ma'qul are either synonymous or different. If the first, then there is no meaningful benefit in mentioning them; if the second, then they are either negative or positive concepts. The first is invalid because we have shown that the meaning of consciousness and perception is not a negative concept, so the second remains, and there is no doubt that they are not different concepts (2) but rather attributes of this essence (3), and then his statement that these three terms do not imply multiplicity in meaning becomes invalid. The second aspect: We have shown that the meaning of consciousness and perception is a relative, relational meaning, so it is impossible for it to be identical to that specific essence. The third aspect: Intellect ('aql) is derived from the occurrence of intellection (ta'aqqul), and its being intelligible to itself is derived from its being intelligent of itself, so these three concepts are derived from one another, so it is impossible for them to be a single concept. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents three objections (wujuh) against an earlier claim that the terms 'aql, 'aqil, and ma'qul do not imply multiplicity. The first objection argues that the three terms are either synonymous or different. If synonymous, mentioning them is redundant. If different, they must be either negative or positive concepts. Since consciousness and perception are positive (not negative), they are attributes of the same essence, contradicting the claim that they do not indicate multiplicity. The second objection states that consciousness is a relational meaning, so it cannot be identical to the essence itself. The third objection shows that these concepts are derived from each other in a sequence (intellect, intellection, being intelligible), so they cannot be one concept. The objections serve to defend the multiplicity of attributes inherent in the essence.

Key Terms
العقل
العاقل
المعقول
مترادفة
متباينة
مفهومات سلبية
مفهومات ثبوتية
شعور
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the three terms are completely unrelated in meaning.
Correction: The objection shows they are related as attributes of the same essence, but still distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, if the three terms are different, what must they be?
A) Negative concepts
B) Positive concepts
C) Synonymous
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The first objection demonstrates that the terms must be distinct positive attributes, contradicting the claim of a single meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat Allah's attributes as just labels, or do you actively let each distinct attribute shape your worship and worldview? Which attribute will you deliberately reflect on this week?
?
The objection shows that logical precision matters in theology. When was the last time you challenged a comfortable assumption about your faith with rigorous thought? What assumption will you re-examine today?
?
If synonymy renders terms useless, what about your own repetitions of Allah's names in duʿā? Are you fully conscious of the distinct meaning of each name you invoke? Commit to learning one new attribute this week and using it with understanding.

Second Objection: Consciousness as a Relative Meaning
Second objection: consciousness is relational, so not identical to essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection, which argues that consciousness is a relational concept and therefore cannot be identical to the essence.

Core Idea
If consciousness is relative, it cannot be identical to the essence itself.

The second objection shifts focus to the relational nature of consciousness and perception. The objector asserts that these concepts are relative (nisbī iḍāfī), meaning they inherently refer to something else. This relational character makes it impossible for them to be identical to the essence, which is absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection passage
Read the short passage in Arabic and English.
Notice the key term 'nisbī iḍāfī'.
2
Understand the claim of relationality
Consciousness and perception are not self-subsistent; they exist only in relation to something else.
3
Grasp the impossibility of identity
Since the essence is absolute (not relational), a relational concept cannot be identical to it.
This directly refutes the claim of unity.
4
Review the checkpoint
Test your understanding with the MCQ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد (۱) ولقائل يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه : الأول : إن لفظ العقل والعاقل والمعقول إما أن تكون ألفاظاً مترادفة أو متباينة . فإن كان الأول لم يكن في ذكرها فائدة معنوية ، وإن كان الثاني فهي إما أن تكون مفهومات سلبية أو ثبوتية. والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك ليس مفهوماً سلبياً ، فبقي الثاني ، ولا شك أنها ليست مفهومات متباينة (۲) بل هي صفات لهذه الذات (۳) ، وحينئذ يبطل قوله : إن هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة لا تفيد كثرة في المعنى . الوجه الثاني : وهو أنا قد دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك معنى نسبي إضافي، فيمتنع كونه عين تلك الذات المخصوصة . الوجه الثالث : إن العقل يتفرع على حصول التعقل ، وكونه معقولاً لنفسه ، يتفرع على كونه عاقلاً لنفسه ، فهذه المفهومات الثلاثة يتفرع بعضها على البعض ، فيمتنع كونها مفهوماً واحداً . والله أعلم.
One (1) and a speaker says: The objection to it is from aspects: First: The terms 'aql, 'aqil, and ma'qul are either synonymous or different. If the first, then there is no meaningful benefit in mentioning them; if the second, then they are either negative or positive concepts. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that consciousness is an inherent attribute without relation.
Correction: This objection emphasizes that consciousness necessarily involves a relation to something else.

Checkpoint
Why does the second objection claim that consciousness cannot be identical to the essence?
A) Because consciousness is a negative concept
B) Because consciousness is relational
C) Because consciousness is temporal
D) Because the essence is multiple

The second objection highlights that the relational nature of consciousness prevents it from being one with the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's consciousness of you is a relational act rather than part of His essence, does that make His awareness of you seem less intimate or more intentional? How does that change the way you approach Him in prayer, knowing He is actively relating to you rather than it being a static fact?
?
When you claim that God knows everything, do you imagine that knowledge as something God 'has' or something He 'does'? This passage argues it is relational — that is, it involves a connection to what is known. How does that affect your trust that He truly engages with your specific situation, not just knows it as a distant fact?
?
This objection forces you to choose: is God's consciousness an essential part of who He is, or is it a relation to creation? Which option makes you feel more accountable for your actions? Why?

Third Objection: Sequential Derivation of Concepts
Third objection: sequential derivation prevents unity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection, which shows that the three concepts are derived from each other in a sequence, making it impossible for them to be a single concept.

Core Idea
Since each concept depends on the others in a chain, they cannot be identical.

The third objection examines the logical relationships among the three terms. The objector argues that intellect (‘aql) is derived from the act of intellection (ta‘aqqul), and being intelligible (ma‘qūl) is derived from being intelligent (‘āqil). This chain of dependency shows that the concepts are distinct and sequential, disproving their unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection passage
Read the passage and translation carefully.
Pay attention to the verb 'تتفرع' (branch/derive).
2
Identify the dependency chain
Notice that ‘aql depends on ta‘aqqul, and ma‘qūl depends on ‘āqil.
This creates a sequence.
3
Understand why this implies distinctness
If they were the same concept, they could not have this ordered dependency.
4
Review the checkpoint
Answer the MCQ to test your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد (۱) ولقائل يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه : الأول : إن لفظ العقل والعاقل والمعقول إما أن تكون ألفاظاً مترادفة أو متباينة . فإن كان الأول لم يكن في ذكرها فائدة معنوية ، وإن كان الثاني فهي إما أن تكون مفهومات سلبية أو ثبوتية. والأول باطل لأنا دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك ليس مفهوماً سلبياً ، فبقي الثاني ، ولا شك أنها ليست مفهومات متباينة (۲) بل هي صفات لهذه الذات (۳) ، وحينئذ يبطل قوله : إن هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة لا تفيد كثرة في المعنى . الوجه الثاني : وهو أنا قد دللنا على أن معنى الشعور والإدراك معنى نسبي إضافي، فيمتنع كونه عين تلك الذات المخصوصة . الوجه الثالث : إن العقل يتفرع على حصول التعقل ، وكونه معقولاً لنفسه ، يتفرع على كونه عاقلاً لنفسه ، فهذه المفهومات الثلاثة يتفرع بعضها على البعض ، فيمتنع كونها مفهوماً واحداً . والله أعلم.
One (1) and a speaker says: The objection to it is from aspects: First: The terms 'aql, 'aqil, and ma'qul are either synonymous or different. If the first, then there is no meaningful benefit in mentioning them; if the second, then they are either negative or positive concepts. The first is invalid because we have shown that the meaning of consciousness and perception is not a negative concept, so the second remains, and there is no doubt that they are not different concepts (2) but rather attributes of this essence (3), and then his statement that these three terms do not imply multiplicity in meaning becomes invalid. The second aspect: We have shown that the meaning of consciousness and perception is a relative, relational meaning, so it is impossible for it to be identical to that specific essence. The third aspect: Intellect ('aql) is derived from the occurrence of intellection (ta'aqqul), and its being intelligible to itself is derived from its being intelligent of itself, so these three concepts are derived from one another, so it is impossible for them to be a single concept. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents three objections (wujuh) against an earlier claim that the terms 'aql, 'aqil, and ma'qul do not imply multiplicity. The first objection argues that the three terms are either synonymous or different. If synonymous, mentioning them is redundant. If different, they must be either negative or positive concepts. Since consciousness and perception are positive (not negative), they are attributes of the same essence, contradicting the claim that they do not indicate multiplicity. The second objection states that consciousness is a relational meaning, so it cannot be identical to the essence itself. The third objection shows that these concepts are derived from each other in a sequence (intellect, intellection, being intelligible), so they cannot be one concept. The objections serve to defend the multiplicity of attributes inherent in the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate Allah's attributes, do you merely accept them as names, or do you struggle to reconcile their logical relationships? This objection demands that you examine whether your concept of divine unity is coherent. Are you willing to confront the intellectual challenges to tawhid, or do you avoid them out of discomfort?
?
The argument shows that sequential derivation prevents conceptual unity. How often do you assume that multiple terms must refer to multiple realities? Could this lesson refine your understanding of how Allah's attributes are distinct yet unified? What would change in your worship if you took this precision seriously?
?
Imagine standing before Allah knowing that you accepted a simplistic view of His attributes without engaging with objections like this. Would that be sufficient? Let this passage push you to dig deeper—commit today to studying one more logical argument about divine attributes.

beginner

The First Premise: God's Knowledge of All Things is Possible
Sanusi presents the overall argument of the mutakallimun and then details the first premise with its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the mutakallimun's argument that God knows all things, and the intuitive proof that establishes its possibility.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of all things is possible because He is living and powerful.

The mutakallimun argue that God knows all things by a three-premise argument. In this lesson, we focus on the first premise: that it is valid (sahh) for God to know every individual thing. The proof is surprisingly simple, relying on intuitive knowledge about life and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three-premise structure and how the first premise is presented.
The overall argument is stated first, then three premises are announced, then the first premise is detailed.
2
Focus on the first premise
Identify the first premise: that it is valid for God to know every one of the infinite known things. Understand that this is about possibility (sihha), not yet necessity.
The proof for this premise uses a logical chain starting from God being knowing and powerful.
3
Trace the proof chain
Follow the reasoning: God is powerful → every powerful being is living → every living being can know all things → this is intuitive → therefore it is not impossible for God to know all.
Each step is based on established or intuitive knowledge.
4
Reflect on the intuitive nature
Notice that the proof ends with 'knowledge of this is intuitive' (بديهي). Reflect on how the mutakallimun rely on self-evident truths.
This makes the argument accessible and foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة : في تقرير دلالة المتكلمين في كونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات قالوا : إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها ، والموجب لكونه تعالى عالماً (٤) هو ذاته المخصوصة ، ومتى كانت الصحة حاصلة في الكل، وكان الموجب هو ذاته المخصوصة وجب أن يكون عالماً بجميع المعلومات . وهذه مقدمات ثلاثة لا بد من تقريرها . المقدمة الأولى : قوله إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات . فالدليل عليه : أنه ثبت أنه تعالي عالم قادر ، وكل من كان قادراً ، فإنه يجب أن يكون حياً ، وكل من كان حياً ، فإنه لا يمتنع عليه أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات، والعلم به بديهي ، ينتج أنه تعالى لا يمتنع كونه عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات
The third issue: On establishing the indication of the mutakallimun that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. They said: It is valid for Him, exalted, to be knowing of every one of the infinite known things, and the necessitator for His being knowing (4) is His specific essence. And when validity is present in all, and the necessitator is His specific essence, then He must be knowing of all known things. These are three premises that must be established. The first premise: His statement that it is valid for Him, exalted, to be knowing of every one of the known things. The proof of it: It is established that He, exalted, is knowing, powerful, and everyone who is powerful must be living, and everyone who is living, it is not impossible for him to be knowing of every one of the known things, and knowledge of this is intuitive. It follows that He, exalted, is not prevented from being knowing of every one of the known things.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage introduces the third issue (al-mas'ala al-thalitha) concerning the mutakallimun's proof that God knows all things. It states the overall argument: it is valid for God to know each infinite particular, and since the cause of knowledge is His essence, He necessarily knows all. Then it announces three premises. The first premise is that it is possible (valid) for God to know every particular. The proof uses the logical chain: God is knowing and powerful; every powerful being must be living; every living being can know all particulars; this is intuitive knowledge. Therefore, it is not impossible for God to know all.

Key Terms
عالم
معلومات
ذات مخصوصة
صحة
قادر
حي
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof directly proves God knows all because He is powerful.
Correction: The proof first establishes possibility (sihha) of knowledge, not necessity. It uses the fact that a powerful being must be living, and a living being can know all—this is intuitive—so it is possible for God to know all. Necessity comes later from the essence being the cause.

Checkpoint
What does the first premise aim to prove?
A) That God must know all things
B) That it is possible for God to know every individual thing
C) That God's essence is the only cause
D) That knowledge is a relation

Key Takeaway
The first premise of the mutakallimun's argument for God's knowledge establishes the possibility of God knowing all things through an intuitive connection between power, life, and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God knows every detail of your life, including your private thoughts. Is there a thought or action you hide from others but cannot hide from Him? Will you bring that to Him in honest admission today?
?
The proof shows that God’s knowledge of all things is possible. Do you truly believe He knows even the smallest details of your day? How would your next hour look if you acted on that belief?
?
You accept that God is knowing and powerful, but how often do you live as if He doesn’t see? What is one specific habit you need to change because you realize you cannot hide from His knowledge?

Second Premise: The Essence as the Cause of Knowledge
Proof that God's essence alone necessitates His knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's essence alone must be the cause of His knowledge, based on the nature of relations and the impossibility of external dependency.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is necessitated only by His essence, not by any external cause.

This lesson examines the second premise of the argument: that the sole cause of God's knowledge is His specific essence. The proof starts by analyzing knowledge as a relation, showing that relations are not self-standing but require a subject. From this, it follows that the attribute of knowledge depends on something else, leading to the conclusion that the cause must be the essence itself, not an external factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to how the argument unfolds step by step.
The passage contains a single coherent proof, so read it as a whole.
2
Understand knowledge as a relation
The proof begins by defining 'being knowing' as a specific relation between the knower and the known. Recognize that this relation is not self-subsistent.
Relations depend on their relata; they are not independent entities.
3
Follow the dependency argument
Since relations are not self-standing, the attribute of knowledge must be an attribute of something else. This makes it an attribute dependent on another, which is possible in itself.
The attribute 'knowledge' in God is not accidental but still requires a ground.
4
Identify the causal alternatives
The argument presents two possibilities for the cause of God's knowledge: His essence (directly or indirectly) or something external. See why the external alternative is rejected.
An external cause would make God dependent on another being, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.
5
Confirm the conclusion
With the external cause eliminated, the only remaining possibility is that the essence itself is the cause. This establishes the second premise.
The conclusion is that God's knowledge is necessitated solely by His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية: فهي قولنا : المقتضى لتلك العالمية ليس إلا ذاته المخصوصة ، والدليل عليه : هو أنا قد بينا : أن كون الشيء عالما بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم ) والنسب لا يمكن أن تكون أموراً قائمة بأنفسها ، والعلم به ضروري . فلا بد وأن تكون صفات لغيرها )(۱) فالعالمية صفة مفتقرة إلى الغير [ والمفتقر إلى الغير (۳) ] ، ممكن لذاته والممكن لذاته ، لا له من سبب . فعالمية الله تعالى لا بد لها من سبب . وذلك السبب إما أن تكون تلك الذات المخصوصة سواء كان بواسطة أو بغير واسطة ، وإما أن تكون غير تلك الذات ، والقسم الثاني باطل . وإلا لزم أن يفتقر في حصول صفة العالمية له تعالى إلى سبب منفصل، وهو محال ، فبقي الأول فيثبت أن المقتضى لكونه تعالى عالماً، ليس إلا ذاته المخصوصة .
As for the second premise: It is our statement: The necessitator for that knowledge is nothing but His specific essence. And the proof of it: We have shown that a thing being knowing of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and relations cannot be self-subsistent things, and knowledge of this is necessary. So they must be attributes of something else (1). Thus knowledge is an attribute dependent on another, and what depends on another (3) is possible in itself, and the possible in itself has no cause from itself. So God's knowledge must have a cause. That cause is either that specific essence, whether by means or without means, or it is other than that essence. The second division is false, for then it would entail that in obtaining the attribute of knowledge for Him, exalted, He depends on a separate cause, which is impossible. So the first remains, establishing that the necessitator for His being knowing, exalted, is nothing but His specific essence.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise, which states that the cause (muqtada) of God's knowledge is His essence alone. The proof begins by considering knowledge as a relation between knower and known. Relations are not self-subsistent; they require a subject. Therefore, the attribute of knowledge is dependent on something else. Since God's knowledge exists, it must have a cause. That cause is either His essence (directly or indirectly) or something external. The external cause is impossible because it would make God dependent on something else. Hence, the cause is His essence itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is uncaused or self-caused without any ground.
Correction: The argument shows that God's knowledge must have a cause, and that cause is His essence itself, not an external factor nor no cause at all.

Checkpoint
Why can the cause of God's knowledge not be external to His essence?
A) Because knowledge is an essential attribute
B) Because it would make God dependent on something else
C) Because relations are self-subsistent
D) Because the universe is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's essence is the sole and sufficient cause of His knowledge; no external factor is involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is solely caused by His essence and depends on nothing external, then what does that say about your constant search for approval from others? Are you willing to find your entire identity in being known by God alone, even if no one else knows you?
?
This passage shows that God's knowledge is not dependent on any external cause. Where in your life are you still acting as if God's knowledge or favor depends on your actions or circumstances? How can you realign your trust to His essence alone?
?
The proof demonstrates that God's essence is the sole cause of His knowledge. Reflect: Do you truly believe that God's knowledge of you is sufficient, or do you feel the need to be 'known' through achievements, status, or relationships? What would it take for you to rest in the sufficiency of His knowledge alone?

God's Knowledge of All Things: The Third Premise
Proof that God must know all things given equal relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument proving that God necessarily knows all things, based on the equality of relation and impossibility of preference.

Core Idea
Equal relation requires equal causation; God's essence must cause knowledge of all.

This lesson presents the third premise in a series, which establishes that God's knowledge is all-encompassing. Building on the possibility of God's knowledge and the fact that His essence is the cause, the argument uses a principle of logical necessity: if the essence relates equally to all knowable things, it cannot cause knowledge of some but not others without a reason. Since no such reason exists, knowledge of all things follows. Read carefully to see how this rational proof unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Notice the flow from the premise to the proof. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical structure
The proof has several steps: (1) possibility of knowledge, (2) essence as cause, (3) equal relation, (4) necessity of all knowledge. Trace these in the text.
Look for key phrases like 'نسبة على السوية' and 'رجحان'.
3
Understand the key principle
The core of the argument is 'الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء غير معقول' – preference when equality obtains is unintelligible. Reflect on why this is a necessary logical truth.
This principle is crucial for many theological arguments.
4
Apply to divine knowledge
Connect the abstract reasoning to the conclusion: because God's essence is equally related to all possible objects of knowledge, and He knows at least one thing, He must know all.
This establishes God's omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثالثة: إنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى يصح أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات ، وثبت أن المقتضى لحصول تلك العالمية هو ذاته المخصوصة وجب الحكم بكونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات . والدليل عليه : أن جميع العالميات صحيحة ، على تلك الذات. والذات موجبة ببعضها، ونسبة تلك الذات إليها بأسرها على السوية ، فلم يكن كونها مقتضية لبعض تلك العالميات ، أولى من كونها مقتضية للبواقي . وقد ثبت أن تلك الذات مقتضية لحصول (3) بعض تلك العالميات ، فوجب أن تكون مقتضية لحصول كلها ، لأن الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء غير معقول ، فيثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى عالماً
As for the third premise: When it is established that He, exalted, can be knowing of every one of the known things, and it is established that the necessitator for obtaining that knowledge is His specific essence, then the judgment must be that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. The proof of it: That all possible objects of knowledge are valid for that essence, and the essence is necessitating of some of them, and the relation of that essence to all of them is equal. So it is not that it is more fitting for it to be necessitating of some of those objects of knowledge than for it to be necessitating of the rest. And it has been established that that essence is necessitating of (3) some of those objects of knowledge, so it must be necessitating of all of them, because preference when equality obtains is unintelligible. Thus it is established that He, exalted, must be knowing.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the third premise, which deduces that God must know all things. Given the first premise (possibility) and the second (essence as cause), the argument proceeds: all possible objects of knowledge are equally related to the essence. The essence already causes knowledge of some objects (since God knows something). Since the relation to all is equal, it cannot cause knowledge of some but not others without a preferential factor. Such preference is impossible when relations are equal. Therefore, the essence must cause knowledge of all objects. Hence God knows all things.

Key Terms

ذات
موجبة
نسبة على السوية
مقتضية
رجحان
استواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could know some things and not others by sheer will, without a reason.
Correction: The argument shows that if the relation is equal, any selection would require a preferrer (مرجح), which is impossible without a reason. Therefore, God must know all things uniformly.

Checkpoint
What is the key principle used in the third premise?
A) The essence is powerful
B) Preference without a preferrer is impossible when relations are equal
C) Knowledge is a relation
D) The universe is created

Key Takeaway
God's essence, being equally related to all knowables and already causing some knowledge, must cause knowledge of all, proving His omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God knows every single thought and intention you have, how would your secret moments change? What would you do differently starting today?
?
This proof shows that God's knowledge of all things is necessary from His essence. How does this affect your understanding of your own relationship with God? Does knowing that His knowledge is essential and unchanging give you more comfort or more fear? Why?
?
You now know that God's knowledge of your entire life is complete and unchangeable. What is one area of your life where you have been acting as if God does not see you? What will you do about it today?

First Premise: Possibility of Omniscience
First premise: proving the possibility of divine omniscience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the proof: that it is possible for God to know every single thing, based on His attributes and the nature of living beings.

Core Idea
God's power and life make omniscience possible.

The author begins by establishing the possibility of God's omniscience, a necessary starting point. He argues from God's known attributes of knowledge and power, deducing that a living being capable of power can also know all things. This self-evident reasoning sets the stage for the deeper causal analysis to follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise and proof
Read the first premise and its proof. Notice how the author uses self-evident truths about power, life, and knowledge.
The proof is deductive: if God is powerful, He must be living; if living, He can know anything.
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument step by step: from God being knowing and powerful, to being living, to not being prevented from knowing anything.
The conclusion is that it is not impossible (i.e., possible) for God to know each thing.
3
Reflect on self-evidence
The author says the connection between life and possibility of knowledge is self-evident. Consider why this is taken as obvious in the kalam tradition.
Self-evident means no further proof is needed for that link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى : قوله إنه تعالى يصح المعلومات . فالدليل عليه : أنه ثبت أنه تعالي عالم قادر ، وكل من كان قادراً ، فإنه يجب أن يكون حياً ، وكل من كان حياً ، فإنه لا يمتنع عليه أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات، والعلم به بديهي ، ينتج أنه تعالى لا يمتنع كونه عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات

وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي قولنا : المقتضى لتلك العالمية ليس إلا ذاته المخصوصة ، والدليل عليه : هو أنا قد بينا : أن كون الشيء عالما بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم ) والنسب لا يمكن أن تكون أموراً قائمة بأنفسها ، والعلم به ضروري . فلا بد وأن تكون صفات لغيرها )(۱) فالعالمية صفة مفتقرة إلى الغير [ والمفتقر إلى الغير (۳) ] ، ممكن لذاته والممكن لذاته ، لا له من سبب . فعالمية الله تعالى لا بد لها من سبب . وذلك السبب إما أن تكون تلك الذات المخصوصة سواء كان بواسطة أو بغير واسطة ، وإما أن تكون غير تلك الذات ، والقسم الثاني باطل . وإلا لزم أن يفتقر في حصول صفة العالمية له تعالى إلى سبب منفصل، وهو محال ، فبقي الأول فيثبت أن المقتضى لكونه تعالى عالماً ، ليس إلا ذاته المخصوصة .

وأما المقدمة الثالثة : إنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى يصح أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات ، وثبت أن المقتضى لحصول تلك العالمية هو ذاته المخصوصة وجب الحكم بكونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات . والدليل عليه : أن جميع العالميات صحيحة ، على تلك الذات. والذات موجبة ببعضها ، ونسبة تلك الذات إليها بأسرها على السوية ، فلم يكن كونها مقتضية لبعض تلك العالميات ، أولى من كونها مقتضية للبواقي . وقد ثبت أن تلك الذات مقتضية لحصول (۳) بعض تلك العالميات ، فوجب أن تكون مقتضية لحصول كلها ، لأن الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء غير معقول ، فيثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها. وهذا تمام تقرير هذا الدليل .
The first premise: His statement that it is possible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing. The proof for it is that it has been established that He, exalted, is knowing, powerful, and everyone who is powerful must be living, and everyone who is living, it is not impossible for him to know each particular thing. This is self-evidently known, resulting that it is not impossible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing. As for the second premise: it is our statement: the cause of that omniscience is nothing but His specific essence. The proof for it is that we have explained that a thing's being knowledgeable of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and relations cannot be self-subsistent things—this is necessarily known. So they must be attributes of others. So omniscience is an attribute that needs another, and what needs another is contingent by itself. The contingent by itself has no cause from itself. So the omniscience of God, exalted, must have a cause. That cause is either that specific essence, whether through mediation or without mediation, or something other than that essence. The second division is false, otherwise it would entail that in obtaining the attribute of omniscience He depends on a separate cause, which is impossible. So the first remains, thus establishing that the cause of His being omniscient is nothing but His specific essence. As for the third premise: Since it has been established that it is possible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing, and it has been established that the cause for obtaining that omniscience is His specific essence, then it is necessary to judge that He, exalted, knows all things. The proof for it is that all instances of omniscience are possible for that essence. The essence necessitates some of them, and the relation of that essence to all of them is equal. So it is not more appropriate for it to necessitate some of those instances of omniscience rather than the others. And it has been established that that essence necessitates obtaining some of those instances of omniscience, so it must necessitate obtaining all of them, because preference when equality obtains is inconceivable. So it is established that He, exalted, must know all things that have no end. This completes the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a structured proof (burhan) composed of three premises. The first premise argues for the possibility of God's omniscience based on His attributes of power and knowledge, and the nature of living beings. The second premise establishes that the cause of omniscience is God's essence alone, by analyzing the nature of attributes and contingency. The third premise concludes necessity: since the essence is equally related to all knowables and already necessitates some knowledge, it must necessitate all, otherwise an arbitrary preference would be irrational. The author follows a logical sequence from possibility to necessity, using arguments from self-evidence and relational analysis.

قادر
حي
بديهي

B) It is possible for God to know each thing

D) God's knowledge is limited

Key Takeaway
The first premise establishes that God's omniscience is at least possible, based on His power and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's omniscience is possible, then every moment of your life is fully known to Him. How does this awareness change the way you view your secret thoughts and intentions? What specific thought or habit will you bring into the light of His knowledge today?
?
The proof uses the self-evident nature of a living being's ability to know. Do you live as if God's knowledge is self-evident to you? When do you act as if He does not see? What will you do to align your actions with this reality starting now?
?
This premise only establishes possibility, not actuality. Yet you believe God knows everything. What is the difference between possibility and actuality in your daily conviction? Are there areas of your life where you doubt that God truly knows the details? How will you strengthen your certainty?

Second Premise: The Cause is God's Essence
Second premise: identifying the cause of divine omniscience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise: that the cause of God's omniscience is nothing but His specific essence, not any external factor.

Core Idea
Omniscience depends solely on God's essence, not on anything else.

After establishing possibility, the author now investigates the cause of omniscience. He argues that omniscience, being an attribute, requires a cause. Through a careful analysis of relations and contingency, he eliminates external causes and concludes that God's own essence must be the sufficient cause. This premise is crucial for moving from possibility to necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of attributes
The author explains that knowing something is a relation, and relations cannot exist by themselves—they need a subject. Omniscience is an attribute dependent on another.
This is a metaphysical principle about relations.
2
Follow the causal argument
Since omniscience is contingent (it needs a cause), God's omniscience must have a cause. The cause must be either God's essence or something external. The latter is impossible.
The impossibility of an external cause is because God cannot depend on something separate.
3
Conclude the premise
Therefore, the cause of God's omniscience is His specific essence alone.
This establishes the 'what' of the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The first premise: His statement that it is possible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing. The proof for it is that it has been established that He, exalted, is knowing, powerful, and everyone who is powerful must be living, and everyone who is living, it is not impossible for him to know each particular thing. This is self-evidently known, resulting that it is not impossible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing. As for the second premise: it is our statement: the cause of that omniscience is nothing but His specific essence. The proof for it is that we have explained that a thing's being knowledgeable of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and relations cannot be self-subsistent things—this is necessarily known. So they must be attributes of others. So omniscience is an attribute that needs another, and what needs another is contingent by itself. The contingent by itself has no cause from itself. The proof for it is that all instances of omniscience are possible for that essence. The essence necessitates some of them, and the relation of that essence to all of them is equal. So it is not more appropriate for it to necessitate some of those instances of omniscience rather than the others. And it has been established that that essence necessitates obtaining some of those instances of omniscience, so it must necessitate obtaining all of them, because preference when equality obtains is inconceivable. So it is established that He, exalted, must know all things that have no end. This completes the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a structured proof (burhan) composed of three premises. The first premise argues for the possibility of God's omniscience based on His attributes of power and knowledge, and the nature of living beings. The second premise establishes that the cause of omniscience is God's essence alone, by analyzing the nature of attributes and contingency. The third premise concludes necessity: since the essence is equally related to all knowables and already necessitates some knowledge, it must necessitate all, otherwise an arbitrary preference would be irrational. The author follows a logical sequence from possibility to necessity, using arguments from self-evidence and relational analysis.

Correction: The author argues that an external cause would imply God's dependence on something separate, which is impossible for God.

A) The objects of knowledge

D) Nothing; it is uncaused

Key Takeaway
The second premise establishes that God's own essence is the sole cause of His omniscience, excluding any external dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof shows that God's omniscience depends on nothing outside Himself. How would your daily life change if you truly believed that God's knowledge of you is not caused by your deeds but by His own perfection? What would you do differently today?
?
This argument is rational, but knowledge without heart transformation is empty. Pause and ask yourself: Do I truly live as if God's all-encompassing knowledge is a reality that covers my private thoughts and hidden intentions? What specific habit or thought pattern will you change because of this truth?

Third Premise: Necessity of Universal Knowledge
Third premise: concluding necessary omniscience from possibility and causal essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third premise: that since omniscience is possible and caused by God's essence, He must actually know all things without exception.

Core Idea
God's essence, equally related to all knowables, necessitates universal knowledge.

The final premise completes the proof by moving from possibility to actual necessity. The author combines the first two premises—possibility and causation by essence—and argues that if the essence is equally related to all objects of knowledge and already necessitates some knowledge, it must necessitate all knowledge. Otherwise, an arbitrary preference would be irrational. This clinches the conclusion that God knows everything.

Guided Path
1
Combine the previous premises
Recall: it is possible for God to be omniscient (premise 1) and the cause is His essence (premise 2). Now we deduce actuality.
The third premise uses the results of the first two.
2
Understand the equality argument
The essence is equally related to all instances of omniscience. If it necessitates some, it must necessitate all, since unequal treatment would require a reason.
This is a principle of rational preference: no preference without a differentiator.
3
Recognize the necessity conclusion
Therefore, God necessarily knows all things without end. This is the final conclusion of the entire proof.
The proof is now complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى : قوله إنه تعالى يصح المعلومات . فالدليل عليه : أنه ثبت أنه تعالي عالم قادر ، وكل من كان قادراً ، فإنه يجب أن يكون حياً ، وكل من كان حياً ، فإنه لا يمتنع عليه أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات، والعلم به بديهي ، ينتج أنه تعالى لا يمتنع كونه عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات

وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي قولنا : المقتضى لتلك العالمية ليس إلا ذاته المخصوصة ، والدليل عليه : هو أنا قد بينا : أن كون الشيء عالما بالشيء نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم ) والنسب لا يمكن أن تكون أموراً قائمة بأنفسها ، والعلم به ضروري . فلا بد وأن تكون صفات لغيرها )(۱) فالعالمية صفة مفتقرة إلى الغير [ والمفتقر إلى الغير (۳) ] ، ممكن لذاته والممكن لذاته ، لا له من سبب . فعالمية الله تعالى لا بد لها من سبب . وذلك السبب إما أن تكون تلك الذات المخصوصة سواء كان بواسطة أو بغير واسطة ، وإما أن تكون غير تلك الذات ، والقسم الثاني باطل . وإلا لزم أن يفتقر في حصول صفة العالمية له تعالى إلى سبب منفصل، وهو محال ، فبقي الأول فيثبت أن المقتضى لكونه تعالى عالماً ، ليس إلا ذاته المخصوصة .

وأما المقدمة الثالثة : إنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى يصح أن يكون عالماً بكل واحد من المعلومات ، وثبت أن المقتضى لحصول تلك العالمية هو ذاته المخصوصة وجب الحكم بكونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات . والدليل عليه : أن جميع العالميات صحيحة ، على تلك الذات. والذات موجبة ببعضها ، ونسبة تلك الذات إليها بأسرها على السوية ، فلم يكن كونها مقتضية لبعض تلك العالميات ، أولى من كونها مقتضية للبواقي . وقد ثبت أن تلك الذات مقتضية لحصول (۳) بعض تلك العالميات ، فوجب أن تكون مقتضية لحصول كلها ، لأن الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء غير معقول ، فيثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها. وهذا تمام تقرير هذا الدليل .
The first premise: His statement that it is possible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing. The proof for it is that it has been established that He, exalted, is knowing, powerful, and everyone who is powerful must be living, and everyone who is living, it is not impossible for him to know each particular thing. This is self-evidently known, resulting that it is not impossible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing. As for the second premise: it is our statement: the cause of that omniscience is nothing but His specific essence. The proof for it is that we have explained that a thing's being knowledgeable of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and relations cannot be self-subsistent things—this is necessarily known. So they must be attributes of others. So omniscience is an attribute that needs another, and what needs another is contingent by itself. The contingent by itself has no cause from itself. So the omniscience of God, exalted, must have a cause. That cause is either that specific essence, whether through mediation or without mediation, or something other than that essence. The second division is false, otherwise it would entail that in obtaining the attribute of omniscience He depends on a separate cause, which is impossible. So the first remains, thus establishing that the cause of His being omniscient is nothing but His specific essence. As for the third premise: Since it has been established that it is possible for Him, exalted, to know each particular thing, and it has been established that the cause for obtaining that omniscience is His specific essence, then it is necessary to judge that He, exalted, knows all things. The proof for it is that all instances of omniscience are possible for that essence. The essence necessitates some of them, and the relation of that essence to all of them is equal. So it is not more appropriate for it to necessitate some of those instances of omniscience rather than the others. And it has been established that that essence necessitates obtaining some of those instances of omniscience, so it must necessitate obtaining all of them, because preference when equality obtains is inconceivable. So it is established that He, exalted, must know all things that have no end. This completes the exposition of this proof.

This passage is a structured proof (burhan) composed of three premises. The first premise argues for the possibility of God's omniscience based on His attributes of power and knowledge, and the nature of living beings. The second premise establishes that the cause of omniscience is God's essence alone, by analyzing the nature of attributes and contingency. The third premise concludes necessity: since the essence is equally related to all knowables and already necessitates some knowledge, it must necessitate all, otherwise an arbitrary preference would be irrational. The author follows a logical sequence from possibility to necessity, using arguments from self-evidence and relational analysis.

Two Objections to the Proof of Omniscience
Author presents two objections to a proof of God's omniscience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical objections raised against the rational proof of God's omniscience.

Core Idea
Objections challenge the proof's logic, not God's attribute.

The previous lesson presented a proof for God's omniscience based on His being living. Now, we encounter two objections that test the logical strength of that proof. These objections do not deny that God is omniscient, but they argue that the proof itself is flawed, either by circular reasoning or by an unwarranted leap from possibility to necessity. Reading these objections carefully helps sharpen your skills in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice that the author presents two distinct objections.
The text ends mid-objection; focus on understanding the structure of each objection.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection claims the proof is circular: it assumes that a living being can know all things, but that is exactly what needs to be proven. The second objection challenges the necessity: even if God could know all things, why must He?
Each objection targets a different premise in the original proof.
3
Analyze the argument against circularity
The objector says the proof merely restates the premise that a living being can know all things, without providing a real argument. This is called a 'sophistry' (مغالطة). Consider whether the proof actually adds anything new.
The objector also raises a dilemma: if the premise is self-evident, just assert it; if not, it needs independent proof.
4
Examine the second objection
The second objection questions why a specific essence that necessitates some omniscience must necessitate all. The objector denies that the relation to all knowables is equal and asks whether the claim is about reality or merely our lack of preference.
This objection touches on the distinction between metaphysical necessity and epistemic indifference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه :
الأول : لا نسلّم أنه يصح كونه تعالى عالماً ، بكل واحد من المعلومات أن قوله : ( إنه قادر عالم ، وكل قادر عالم فإنه حي ، وكل حي ، فإنه يصح يعلم كل واحد واحد من المعلومات ، قلنا : هذا مغالطة. لأن معنى الحي : أنه الذي يصح أن يعلم ويقدر فقوله : كل قادر عالم فإنه حي - معناه : أن كل قادر عالم، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك القدرة، وبذلك العلم. فلم قلتم : إن كل من لا يمتنع أن يكون موصوفاً بالقدرة المخصوصة وبالعلم المخصوص ، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون موصوفاً بسائر العلوم ؟ فإن كان قولنا : إن كل من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم سائر المعلومات . مقدمة بديهية ، فاتركوا هذا الدليل ، واكتفوا بدعوى البديهة. وإن كانت مقدمة نظرية ، فلا بد في تقريرها من الدليل . وما ذكرتموه وإن كان يوهم أنه دليل إلا أن البحث الذي ذكرناه ظهر أنه إعادة تلك المقدمة بعبارة أخرى .
والسؤال الثاني : هب سلمنا أن تلك الذات المخصوصة لا يمتنع عليها كونها عالمة بسائر المعلومات - فلم قلتم : إن يجب كونها عالمة بسائر المعلومات ؟ قوله : تلك الذات المخصوصة هي الموجبة لتلك العالمية ( ونسبة تلك الذات الموجبة لتلك العالمية ( ) إلى سائر العلوم ( على التسوية )) قلنا : هذا ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : تلك الذات المخصوصة توجب لذاتها بعض العالميات دون بعض ؟ وقوله : «ليس كونها موجبة لبعض تلك العالميات أولى من كونها موجبة لسائرها (4) ، كلام ضعيف . لانا نقول : ندعي(ه) أنه ليس بعضها أولى من البعض في نفس الأمر ، أو تدعي عدم هذه الأولوية في عقولنا وأذهاننا ؟
And someone may say: The objection to it is from [several] aspects: The first: We do not grant that it is possible for Him, exalted, to be knowing of each particular thing through His statement: 'He is powerful, knowing, and every powerful knowing being is living, and every living being can know each individual thing.' We say: This is sophistry. Because the meaning of 'living' is that which can know and can will. So His statement 'every powerful knowing being is living' means that every powerful knowing being is not prevented from being described with that power and that knowledge. So why do you say that everyone who is not prevented from being described with specific power and specific knowledge, then he is not prevented from being described with all sciences? If our statement 'Everyone who knows a thing can know all known things' is a self-evident premise, then abandon this proof and suffice with the claim of self-evidence. But if it is a theoretical premise, then its establishment requires a proof. What you have mentioned, though it suggests it is a proof, the investigation we have made shows that it is just a restatement of that premise in other words. The second objection: Suppose we grant that that specific essence is not prevented from being omniscient of all known things—why do you say that it must be omniscient of all known things? His statement: 'That specific essence is the necessitation of that omniscience, and the relation of that essence that necessitates that omniscience to all sciences is equal'—we say: This is denied. Why is it not permissible that that specific essence necessitates by itself some instances of omniscience and not others? His statement 'It is not more appropriate for it to necessitate some of those instances of omniscience rather than the rest' is a weak statement. Because we say: do you claim that some of them are not more appropriate than some in reality, or do you claim the absence of this preferability in our intellects and minds?
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the previous proof of God's omniscience. The first objection challenges the first premise: the proof that God can know all things based on His being living is accused of circularity, because the definition of 'living' does not entail universal knowledge. The objector suggests the premise is either self-evident (then just assert it) or theoretical (then requires proof), and the proof merely restates it. The second objection challenges the third premise: even if the essence can know all things, why must it? The objector denies that the essence's relation to all knowables is equal, arguing that the essence might necessitate some knowledges and not others, and that the claim of no preferability is ambiguous—whether it asserts ontological equality or epistemic lack of preference. The objections are left without an answer in this passage.

بديهية
نظرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections deny that God knows all things.
Correction: The objections do not deny God's omniscience; they challenge the logical sufficiency of the proof. The objectors may accept omniscience as a matter of faith or other evidence.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot be living
B) The proof's reasoning is circular and does not establish the premise
C) The proof is self-evident and therefore valid
D) The second premise is false

Key Takeaway
The two objections critique the logical soundness of the proof, demanding either self-evidence or a proper argument for the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite that Allah knows all things, do you say it because you have examined the proof and found it sound, or because you were taught it? If a logical objection were raised against it, would you be able to defend it, or would you dismiss the objection without understanding? How can you take personal responsibility to know why you believe what you believe?
?
The objector in this passage asks whether the necessity of God's omniscience is true in reality or only in our minds. This forces a distinction between what is actually the case and what we can prove. Do you live as though your certainty about Allah is grounded in sound reasoning that answers every challenge, or do you rely on comfortable familiarity? What would it cost you to have your faith intellectually fortified this week?

First Objection: Capacity vs. Actual Knowledge
Razi refutes the first objection to the proof of God's omniscience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the proof of God's omniscience and how Razi refutes it by distinguishing between self-evident and theoretical premises.

Core Idea
Capacity to know does not automatically imply actual knowledge of everything; the inference requires proof.

Razi has just presented a proof that God knows all particulars. Now he anticipates an objection: does being powerful and knowing necessarily entail knowing every single thing? The objector argues that it does not, and Razi responds by exposing the flawed logic. This lesson walks through that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first objection and Razi's refutation. Pay attention to the objector's claim and the meaning of 'living' (hayy).
2
Identify the objector's reasoning
The objector denies that being powerful and knowing automatically implies knowing every single particular. He argues that 'living' is defined as capable of knowledge and power, not necessarily all knowledge.
Note the shift from possibility to actuality.
3
Analyze Razi's response
Razi points out that the objector's 'proof' merely restates the disputed premise. He then distinguishes between a self-evident (badihi) and a theoretical (nazari) premise, showing that the objector fails to provide a genuine proof.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Consider how this distinction between self-evident and theoretical is used to refute the objection. Why is the objector's claim not self-evident?
This illuminates the proper method of theological reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

With all the infinite particulars. And this is the complete exposition of this proof. And someone may say: There are objections to it from several aspects: First: We do not grant that it is valid that He, exalted, is knowing every one of the particulars—that is, His saying: 'He is powerful, knowing, and every powerful knower is living, and every living one it is valid that he knows every single particular'—we say: This is a fallacy. Because the meaning of 'living' is that which it is valid that he knows and has power. So His saying 'every powerful knower is living' means: every powerful knower is such that it is not impossible that he be described with that power and that knowledge. So why do you say: that everyone for whom it is not impossible to be described with specific power and specific knowledge, it is not impossible for him to be described with all other knowledges? If our saying 'everyone who knows one thing can know all other objects of knowledge' is a self-evident premise, then abandon this proof and content yourselves with claiming self-evidence. And if it is a theoretical premise, then its exposition requires a proof. And what you have mentioned, even if it gives the illusion that it is a proof, the investigation we have mentioned shows that it is a restatement of that premise in other words. And the second objection: Grant that we concede that that specific essence is not impossible to be knowing all objects of knowledge, why do you say that it must be knowing all objects of knowledge? His statement: That specific essence is the necessitator of that omniscience, and the relation of that essence necessitating that omniscience to all objects of knowledge is uniform. We say: This is denied. So why is it not permissible to say that that specific essence necessitates by itself some knowledges and not others? And his statement: 'It is not that its being a necessitator of some of those knowledges is more likely than its being a necessitator of all of them' is a weak statement. For we say: Do we claim that some of them are more likely than others in reality, or do you claim the lack of this priority in our minds and intellects? The first is denied. That is because knowledge of this object of knowledge differs from knowledge of that second object of knowledge, and it does not follow from an essence being a necessitator of something that it is a necessitator of what differs from that thing, because different realities are not required to be equal in rulings and effects. And the second—namely, the lack of priority in our minds and thoughts—is conceded, but this does not benefit, except that the intellect halts in this judgment and does not decide about it anything at all. As for deciding based on lack of disparity, that is false. Do you not see that the theologians say: Knowledge is an attribute related to the known? Then they claimed that knowledge of each object has a quiddity that differs from the quiddity of knowledge related to another object. That particular knowledge is a specific quiddity that requires, by its particular quiddity, relation to that particular known to the exclusion of all others. And if this is understood regarding knowledge, then why is it not also permissible to say: That specific essence necessitates by itself relation to some objects of knowledge and not others? So this is the sum of the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the proof that God knows all particular things, along with their refutations. The author first completes his earlier proof, then introduces the objector. The first objection challenges the inference from 'God is powerful and knowing' to 'He can know every particular'. The objector claims that being living (hayy) does not automatically entail knowing all things. The author responds that the meaning of 'living' is simply that it is not impossible to have power and knowledge; the objector's argument merely restates the premise without proof. The second objection concedes that God's essence might be able to know all, but questions why it must. The objector argues that if the essence is a necessitator of omniscience, then the relation to all objects is uniform. The author denies this, pointing out that different types of knowledge have different quiddities, and the essence may necessitate some but not others. He uses the theologians' own view that each knowledge is specific to its object to support this. Thus both objections fail, and the author concludes the chapter with 'Wa-llahu a'lam'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God can know one thing, He must know all, and that the inference is automatic.
Correction: Razi shows that the inference from capacity to actual knowledge is not self-evident and requires proof; the objector's argument fails to provide such proof.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about the premise 'everyone who knows one thing can know all objects of knowledge'?
A) It is self-evident and requires no proof.
B) It is theoretical and requires a proof, which the objector fails to provide.
C) It is false because no one can know all things.
D) It is only true for God.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because the objector confuses a claim of possibility with a claim of necessity, and his 'proof' merely restates the disputed premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to know something about God, do you ever confuse your capacity to know (because you are a human with intellect) with actually possessing that knowledge? Where in your faith have you stopped investigating, assuming that because you could know, you already do? Today, pick one attribute of God you think you understand and challenge yourself to find a proof from revelation or reason—don't settle for restating what you've always heard.
?
Razi exposes the trap of mistaking a restatement for a proof. Have you ever done that in your own beliefs—repeated a phrase like 'Allah knows everything' without truly grasping why it must be so? Are you willing to examine your most deeply held convictions to see if they are supported by genuine evidence or just comfortable repetition? Commit this week to questioning one belief you've never doubted and seeking a real proof.
?
The objector relies on a self-evident premise to avoid providing a proof. What claim about God do you treat as 'obvious' or 'common sense' without ever subjecting it to rigorous thought? Could it be that you are avoiding the intellectual effort required to understand your faith more deeply? Will you take one hour this week to study a difficult theological question instead of assuming the answer is clear?

Second Objection: Uniformity of Relation and Quiddity of Knowledge
Razi refutes the second objection concerning the uniformity of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against God's omniscience and Razi's response based on the diversity of knowledge quiddities.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge is alike; the specific quiddity of each knowledge makes it unique, so an essence may necessitate some but not all.

The second objection grants that God's essence could know all particulars but questions why it must. The objector claims that if the essence necessitates omniscience, then it must do so uniformly for all objects. Razi refutes this by appealing to the nature of knowledge itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the second objection and Razi's refutation. Focus on the objector's claim of uniform relation and Razi's two-part response.
2
Identify the objector's argument
The objector says: if God's essence is the necessitator of omniscience, then its relation to all objects of knowledge is uniform, so it must know all equally.
3
Understand Razi's denial of uniform necessity
Razi rejects the uniform relation premise. He asks whether the priority of some knowledges over others is in reality or in our minds. The first is denied because different knowledges have different quiddities; the second is conceded but irrelevant.
4
See how Razi uses the quiddity of knowledge
Razi cites the theologians' view that knowledge of each object has a distinct quiddity. Therefore, just as each knowledge relates specifically to its object, God's essence can necessitate knowledge of some things without necessitating others.

Source Text

معناه : أن كل قادر عالم، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك القدرة، ويذلك العلم. فلم قلتم : إن كل من لا يمتنع أن يكون موصوفاً بالقدرة المخصوصة وبالعلم المخصوص ، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون موصوفاً بسائر العلوم ؟ فإن كان قولنا : إن كل من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم سائر المعلومات . مقدمة بديهية ، فاتركوا هذا الدليل ، واكتفوا بدعوى البديهة. وإن كانت مقدمة نظرية ، فلا بد في تقريرها من الدليل . وما ذكرتموه وإن كان يوهم أنه دليل إلا أن البحث الذي ذكرناه ظهر أنه إعادة تلك المقدمة بعبارة أخرى . والسؤال الثاني : هب (۱) سلمنا أن تلك الذات المخصوصة لا يمتنع عليها كونها عالمة بسائر المعلومات - With all the infinite particulars. And this is the complete exposition of this proof. And someone may say: There are objections to it from several aspects: First: We do not grant that it is valid that He, exalted, is knowing every one of the particulars—that is, His saying: 'He is powerful, knowing, and every powerful knower is living, and every living one it is valid that he knows every single particular'—we say: This is a fallacy. So His saying 'every powerful knower is living' means: every powerful knower is such that it is not impossible that he be described with that power and that knowledge. The Argument from Perfection and Caution
Razi presents a simple argument that God must know all because ignorance is imperfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's simple yet powerful argument that God must know all things because ignorance is an imperfection and affirming omniscience is the cautious position in theology.

Core Idea
Perfection leaves no room for ignorance; caution demands we affirm God's omniscience.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a straightforward argument for God's omniscience that appeals to two principles: divine perfection and theological caution. This passage shows how rational necessity and religious prudence converge on the same conclusion. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation. Notice the two parts: the main argument from perfection and the additional appeal to caution. The author uses 'and also' to introduce the second point.
The Arabic is concise; every word matters.
2
Understand the perfection argument
Identify the three premises: God is the most perfect being; ignorance is a deficiency; reason requires God to be free from deficiency. The conclusion follows necessarily: God knows all things.
This is a deductive syllogism.
3
Understand the caution argument
The author adds that affirming omniscience is the safer (more cautious) position. Consider why it might be safer: it avoids attributing imperfection to God and aligns with religious teachings.
Caution here means erring on the side of affirming an attribute that is clearly positive.
4
Reflect on the combination
Notice that the two arguments work together: the first is rational necessity, the second is pragmatic prudence. Both point to the same truth.
Razi often uses multiple arguments to strengthen a position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأولى في هذا الباب . أن نقول : لا شك أنه تعالى أكمل الموجودات والجهل بأي معلوم كان ، صفة نقص . وصريح العقل يقضي بوجوب تنزيه الله عن النقص . فوجب الحكم بكونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات . وأيضاً : فهذا القول أقرب إلى الاحتياط ، فوجب المصير إليه
And the most appropriate in this chapter is to say: There is no doubt that He, exalted, is the most perfect of existents, and ignorance of any object whatsoever is an attribute of deficiency. And clear reason dictates the necessity of declaring God free from deficiency. So it is necessary to judge that He, exalted, is knowing all objects of knowledge. Also, this statement is closer to caution, so it is necessary to adopt it.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage provides a concise and decisive argument for God's omniscience. The author states that the best approach in this chapter is to rely on two points. First, God is the most perfect being (akmal al-mawjudat). Ignorance of any thing is a deficiency (naqs). Reason dictates that God must be free from any deficiency. Therefore, He must know all things. Second, this conclusion is also the safer option (aqrab ila al-ihtiyat) in matters of theology, so it must be adopted. The argument is straightforward: perfection excludes ignorance, so God is omniscient.

Key Terms
أكمل الموجودات
جهل
صفة نقص
تنزيه
احتياط
عالم بجميع المعلومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's omniscience is known only through revelation, not reason.
Correction: Razi shows that reason alone, through the principle of perfection and the principle of caution, necessitates God's omniscience. Revelation confirms it, but reason already demands it.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, why must God be omniscient?
A) Because scripture says so.
B) Because ignorance is a deficiency and God is perfect.
C) Because it is safer to believe in omniscience.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves God's omniscience through two complementary arguments: rational necessity from divine perfection and prudence from theological caution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You claim to believe that God is most perfect. But do you live as if His omniscience is real? When you sin in secret, are you not acting as if God does not know? Reflect on that gap and resolve to close it.
