Refuting an Objection Against Essential Knowledge
Refutes an objection against essential knowledge by using the mutakallimun's own definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes an objection against divine essential knowledge by appealing to the mutakallimun's definition of knowledge as an attribute related to a known.

Core Idea
If each knowledge-essence necessitates its own known, then the divine essence can too.

The objection claims that if God's knowledge is essential, it cannot explain why He knows some things and not others. The author first refutes the objection's premise, then concedes a point but shows it is irrelevant, and finally uses the very definition of knowledge given by the mutakallimun to turn their own argument against them. This lesson walks through each move.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the author's argumentative structure: refutation, concession, then positive argument.
The passage is dense—pause after each sentence.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The author refers to 'the first' and 'the second'. Determine what these correspond to in the context of the objection against essential knowledge.
Look for phrases like 'the first is prohibited' and 'the second is conceded'.
3
Understand the refutation of the first part
The author argues that knowledge of different objects differs, so an essence necessitating one thing does not necessitate its opposite. Why is this a valid refutation?
The key concept is that diverse realities (haqa'iq) are not required to be equal in rulings.
4
Unpack the analogy from the mutakallimun
The author draws an analogy from the mutakallimun's own definition of knowledge. Trace how he uses their framework to support his own position.
Focus on the shift: from knowledge as attribute to essence as necessitating relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأول ممنوع. وذلك لأن العلم بهذا المعلوم يخالف العلم بذلك المعلوم الثاني، ولا يلزم من كون الذات موجبة لشيء كونها موجبة لما يخالف ذلك الشيء. لأن الحقائق المختلفة لا يجب استواؤها في الأحكام والآثار. والثاني: وهو عدم الأولوية في أذهاننا وأفكارنا، مسلم، إلا أن هذا لا يفيد. إلا أن العقل يتوقف في هذا الحكم، ولا يجزم فيه بشيء البتة. فأما أن يجزم فيه لعدم التفاوت فهو باطل. ألا ترى أن المتكلمين يقولون: إن العلم صفة متعلقة بالمعلوم؟ ثم زعموا: أن العلم بكل معلوم ماهيته مخالفة لماهية العلم المتعلق بالمعلوم الآخر. فذلك العلم الخصوص ماهية مخصوصة تقتضي لماهيتها المخصوصة التعلق بذلك المعلوم المعين دون سائر ما سواه. وإذا عقل هذا في العلم فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال: تلك الذات المخصوصة يوجب لذاتها التعلق ببعض المعلومات دون البعض؟ فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الباب. والله أعلم
The first is prohibited. That is because knowledge of this known thing differs from knowledge of that second known thing, and it does not follow from the essence being necessitating of something that it is necessitating of what contradicts that thing. Because diverse realities are not required to be equal in rulings and effects. And the second: which is the lack of priority in our minds and thoughts, is conceded, except that this does not avail. Rather the intellect is suspended in this judgment and does not decide anything at all. As for its deciding due to lack of disparity, that is false. Do you not see that the theologians (mutakallimun) say: knowledge is an attribute related to the known? Then they claimed: knowledge of every known has an essence different from the essence of knowledge related to another known. So that particular knowledge is a particular essence that necessitates by its particularity relation to that particular known to the exclusion of everything else. If this is understood regarding knowledge, why should it not also be said: that particular essence necessitates by its essence relation to some known things to the exclusion of others? This is the sum of the discourse on this topic. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection against the view that God's knowledge is essential. The author begins by stating that the first part of the objection is invalid because knowledge of different objects differs, so an essence that necessitates one thing does not necessitate its opposite. The second part of the objection—that there is no priority in our minds—is conceded but deemed irrelevant; the intellect remains undecided, and claiming that it decides due to lack of disparity is false. The author then appeals to the theologians' (mutakallimun) own account of knowledge: they define knowledge as an attribute related to the known, and they hold that knowledge of each known has a distinct essence. If a particular knowledge-essence necessitates relation to a particular known, then by analogy a particular divine essence could also necessitate relation to some known things rather than others. This argument turns the opponent's own framework against them.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
الذات
الحقائق المختلفة
المتكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author fully concedes the second objection, meaning the objection has some validity.
Correction: The author concedes the fact that there is no priority in our minds, but he argues this does not benefit the objector because the intellect is suspended; the objector cannot draw a conclusion from that lack of priority.

Checkpoint
How does the author use the mutakallimun's definition of knowledge to rebut the objection?
A) He denies that knowledge is an attribute related to the known.
B) He argues that if each particular knowledge has a unique essence that necessitates relation to a particular known, then a particular divine essence can also necessitate relation to specific knowns.
C) He claims that all knowledge is identical, so the objection fails.
D) He asserts that the objection is based on an invalid analogy.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes an objection against essential knowledge by showing that the mutakallimun's own definition of knowledge implies that a particular essence can necessitate relation to specific objects, thereby supporting the doctrine of essential knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of God's knowledge as simply 'knowing everything'. But this lesson forces you to consider that His knowledge of each thing—including your own specific sins, your secret thoughts, your unique circumstances—is directly related to His essence. How does that make you feel about the depth of His awareness of you? If His knowledge of your particular evil deed is as 'particular' as a knowledge-essence must be, how should that change your approach to repentance tonight?
?
The objection raised in this lesson suggests that if God's knowledge is His essence, then He should know all things equally or not at all—implying that God is distant and indifferent. Have you ever harbored the feeling that God is just some 'cosmic knower' who doesn't care about details? The refutation shows that the one essence can necessitate different relations. How does this answer the feeling of indifference? Will you trust that His knowledge of your struggles is as unique as you are, and live accordingly this week?
?
The mutakallimun defined knowledge as an attribute related to the known—an impersonal definition. But the author uses it to argue that the divine essence itself relates to specific knowns. If God's essence is the basis of His knowledge of you, what does that imply about your standing before Him? Are you merely an object of knowledge, or does His essential knowledge of you give you a kind of intimate significance? What will you do today to live in light of that significance?

God's Omniscience: The Perfection and Caution Argument
Presents a preferred argument for God's omniscience from perfection and caution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part preferred argument for God's omniscience based on His perfection and the principle of caution.

Core Idea
God's perfect knowledge is demanded by His perfection and by caution.

In this passage, the author presents his preferred argument for establishing that God knows all things. Rather than a strict logical proof, it combines a rational demonstration from God's perfection with a methodological principle of caution. This reflects the balanced approach of kalam reasoning, where both rational necessity and prudential consideration are taken into account.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of perfection
Recognize that the argument starts with the premise that God is the most perfect of all existents. This is a foundational claim about God's nature.
Perfection here implies freedom from all deficiencies.
2
Infer the necessity of knowledge
Since ignorance of anything is a deficiency, and God must be free from any deficiency, it follows necessarily that God knows all things.
This step uses the rational principle: the most perfect being cannot lack knowledge.
3
Observe the prudential reason
The author adds that affirming God's knowledge of all things is the more cautious (al-ihtiyat) position, making it preferable from a methodological standpoint as well.
This reflects a conservative approach in theological matters: it is safer to attribute full knowledge to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأولى في هذا الباب. أن نقول: لا شك أنه تعالى أكمل الموجودات والجهل بأي معلوم كان، صفة نقص. وصريح العقل يقضي بوجوب تنزيه الله عن النقص. فوجب الحكم بكونه تعالى عالماً بجميع المعلومات. وأيضاً: فهذا القول أقرب إلى الاحتياط، فوجب المصير إليه
The preferred view in this topic is to say: There is no doubt that He, exalted, is the most perfect of existents, and ignorance of any known thing is an attribute of deficiency. And clear reason judges the necessity of declaring God transcendent of deficiency. So it is necessary to judge that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. Also, this view is closer to caution, so it must be adopted.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the author's preferred argument for establishing God's omniscience. It begins by stating the preferred view in this topic. The argument has two parts. First, a perfection-based argument: God is the most perfect of all existents (أكمل الموجودات), and ignorance of anything is a deficiency (نقص). Since reason demands that God be free from any deficiency, He must know all things. Second, a prudential argument: the view that God knows everything is the more cautious (أقرب إلى الاحتياط) position, so it must be adopted. This reflects a methodological preference for 'al-ihtiyat' (caution) in theological matters.

Correction: The passage explicitly presents the argument as the 'preferred' view, combining rational necessity with the principle of caution (al-ihtiyat). It is not a strict proof but a preferred argument that takes both reason and methodological caution into account.

Key Takeaway

?
The lesson argues that ignorance is a deficiency that cannot be attributed to God. But reflect on yourself: where are you content with ignorance—about your own faults, about the rights of others, about the commands of your Lord? How does your willingness to remain in the dark reveal a comfort with deficiency that contradicts the very perfection you affirm for God? In your own life, do you tend to take the cautious route in matters of faith, or do you often cut corners assuming God does not see? Where are you taking the risk of ignoring His knowledge, and what would it cost you to adopt the cautious, God-aware path instead? Make a concrete commitment to one change today.

Opponent's First Objection: Knowledge Cannot Be Identical to Essence
Presents the opponent's first specious argument with five reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's first specious argument against divine knowledge being identical to God's essence, and the five reasons they give to support this.

Core Idea
The opponent argues that God's knowledge must be distinct from His essence, or else He cannot be knowing.

We now turn to the opponent's objections. They first challenge the very idea that God is knowing. Their first specious argument is a dilemma: if God knows, His knowledge is either identical to His essence or distinct. They claim both are impossible, so God cannot be knowing. To prove that identity is impossible, they present five reasons. This lesson examines this argument and its five supporting points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get a sense of the opponent's argument.
The text ends mid-sentence; the fifth reason is incomplete.
2
Identify the two opponent positions
The passage begins by stating the opponent has two positions: disputing that God is knowing (العالِميّة), and disputing that He knows all things. Focus on the first position.
3
Analyze the dilemma structure
The opponent sets up a dilemma: God's knowledge is either identical to His essence or other than it. Both are claimed false, so 'God is knowing' is false. Notice the claim that identity is impossible is supported by five reasons.
4
Examine the five reasons against identity
Go through each of the five reasons one by one. Note the different approaches: linguistic, logical, propositional, conceptual, and definitional.
The fifth reason is cut off; we have only its beginning.
5
Reflect on the opponent's strategy
Ask yourself: Why does the opponent use a dilemma? What is the purpose of these five reasons? Remember, these are presented as specious arguments (شبهات) to be refuted later.

Source Text

And know: that this opponent has two positions: one: that he disputes the principle of being knowing (al-‘ālimiyya). And the second: those who dispute that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. As for the first position, they have mentioned several specious arguments. The first specious argument: They said: If He were knowing of something, then His being knowing would either be identical to His essence or other than His essence, and both divisions are false, so the consequent is false. As for the division, it is clear. As for it being impossible that His knowledge of known things is identical to His essence, that is due to several aspects: First: We know by necessity that our statement 'Necessary Existent' does not convey anything, while our statement 'Necessary Existent is knowing' conveys a composite meaning, and were it not for the difference, no distinction would occur. Second: The negation of our statement 'He is necessary of existence by Himself' is that He is not necessary of existence. And the negation of our statement 'He is knowing' is that He is not knowing. It is known by necessity that our statement 'He is knowing' is not contradicted by our statement 'He is not necessary of existence', and our statement 'He is necessary of existence' is not contradicted by our statement 'He is not knowing', and were it not that the concept of our statement 'He is knowing' is not the same as the concept of our statement 'He is necessary of existence by Himself', these judgments would not be sound. Third: If we say 'Necessary Existent is knowing', this is a comprehensible proposition, capable of being affirmed or denied in doubt and uncertainty. And every proposition whose predicate is other than its subject is not like that. Fourth: It is that we can conceive His being necessary of existence by Himself and we can conceive His being knowing while doubting His being necessary of existence by Himself. That necessitates difference, for what is known is different from what is not known. Fifth: We have shown that knowledge is expression of a particular relation and a particular connection, and His essence, exalted, is a self-subsistent essence, and it is known by necessity that the essence...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage introduces the two positions of the opponent regarding divine knowledge: first, disputing God's being knowing at all; second, disputing that He knows all things. It then presents the first objection (الشبهة الأولى) from the opponent's first position. The objection is structured as a dilemma: God's knowledge is either identical to His essence or distinct; both are claimed false, so the premise that God is knowing is false. The author then lists five arguments to support that knowledge cannot be identical to the essence. (1) The phrase 'Necessary Existent' conveys no meaning, while 'Necessary Existent is knowing' conveys a composite meaning, indicating difference. (2) The negations are different: 'not necessary' does not contradict 'knowing', and vice versa, so their concepts differ. (3) The proposition 'Necessary Existent is knowing' has a subject and predicate; if they were identical, the proposition would not be informative. (4) Conceptual distinction: we can conceive God's necessary existence without conceiving His knowledge, indicating they are not the same. (5) Knowledge is defined as a particular relation, whereas the essence is self-subsistent; thus they differ. The fifth argument is incomplete as the text ends.

C) It attempts to prove that God's knowledge is created.

The opponent's first objection against God's knowledge rests on the claim that knowledge must be distinct from essence, supported by five arguments showing identity is impossible.

?

The First Two Proofs That Knowledge Is Not Identical to Essence
The first two proofs against identity of knowledge and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first two proofs demonstrate that God's knowledge cannot be identical to His essence through the addition of meaning and the difference in contradictions.

Core Idea
Knowledge adds meaning and has a different contradiction than essence, proving distinctness.

The author begins by establishing the exhaustive division: knowledge is either identical or other than essence. He then presents two initial proofs focusing on linguistic and logical concepts. The first proof shows that the phrase 'Necessary Existence is knowing' conveys additional meaning compared to 'Necessary Existence' alone, implying that knowledge is not the essence. The second proof examines the contradictions of each statement, showing they are not interchangeable, which also indicates distinctness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the exhaustive division
Read the opening lines where the author states that knowledge is either identical to the essence or other than it, then says both are false. This sets up the need for proofs.
The division is exhaustive; there is no third possibility.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof uses the difference in meaning between 'Necessary Existence' and 'Necessary Existence is knowing'. Notice that the latter adds meaning, which would not happen if they were identical.
Focus on the phrase 'لا يفيد شيئاً' compared to 'يفيد معنى منظوماً'.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof looks at contradictions. The contradictory of 'He is Knowing' is different from the contradictory of 'He is Necessary Existence'. Moreover, these statements do not contradict each other's negations, which shows they are distinct concepts.
Highlight the logical relation: 'لا يناقضه' indicates non-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون عين ذاته، وإما أن يكون غير ذاته، والقسمان باطلان فبطل الملزوم. أما الحصر فظاهر. وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون علمه بالمعلومات عين ذاته فلوجوه: الأول: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن قولنا: واجب الوجود فإنه لا يفيد شيئاً. وقولنا: واجب الوجود عالم يفيد معنى منظوماً، ولولا التغاير لما حصل الفرق. الثاني: إن نقيض قولنا: إنه واجب الوجود لذاته هو أنه ليس واجب الوجود. ونقيض قولنا: إنه عالم هو أنه ليس بعالم. ومعلوم بالضرورة أن قولنا: إنه عالم لا يناقضه قولنا إنه ليس واجب الوجود. وقولنا: إنه واجب الوجود لا يناقضه قولنا: إنه ليس بعالم، ولولا أن المفهوم من قولنا: إنه عالم ليس نفس المفهوم من قولنا: إنه واجب الوجود لذاته لما صحت هذه الأحكام. الثالث: إنا إذا قلنا: واجب الوجود عالم. فهذه قضية مفهومة، قابلة للتصديق والتكذيب في الشك والشبهة. وكل قضية يكون محمولها غير موضوعها، فإنها لا تكون كذلك. الرابع: وهو أنه يمكننا تعقل كونه واجب الوجود لذاته مع الشك في كونه عالماً، ويمكننا تعقل كونه عالماً مع الشك في كونه واجب الوجود لذاته. وذلك يوجب التغاير فإن المعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم. الخامس: أنا بينا أن العلم عبارة عن التعلق المخصوص وعن النسبة المخصوصة، وذاته تعالى ذات قائمة بنفسها، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن الذات القائمة بنفسها مغايرة للنسبة المخصوصة، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه يمتنع أن يكون علمه عين ذاته.
It would be identical to His essence, or it would be other than His essence, and both divisions are false, so the underlying premise is false. As for the exhaustive division, it is obvious. As for that it is impossible for His knowledge of things to be identical to His essence, there are proofs: First: We know necessarily that our statement 'Necessary Existence' does not convey any meaning [beyond existence], and our statement 'Necessary Existence is knowing' conveys an additional meaning, and were it not for distinctness, no difference would arise. Second: The contradictory of our statement 'He is Necessary Existence in Himself' is 'He is not Necessary Existence,' and the contradictory of our statement 'He is knowing' is 'He is not knowing.' And it is known necessarily that our statement 'He is knowing' does not contradict our statement 'He is not Necessary Existence,' and our statement 'He is Necessary Existence' does not contradict our statement 'He is not knowing.' And were it not that the concept from our statement 'He is knowing' is not the same concept from our statement 'He is Necessary Existence in Himself,' these judgments would not be correct. Third: If we say 'Necessary Existence is knowing,' this is an intelligible proposition, capable of being affirmed or denied in doubt and ambiguity. Any proposition whose predicate is not identical to its subject is not [capable of this]. Fourth: It is possible for us to conceive Him as Necessary Existence in Himself while doubting that He is knowing, and it is possible for us to conceive Him as knowing while doubting that He is Necessary Existence in Himself. This necessitates distinctness, for the known is distinct from that which is not known. Fifth: We have shown that knowledge is an expression of a particular relation and a particular attribute, and His exalted essence is an essence subsistent by itself, and it is known necessarily that the essence subsistent by itself is distinct from the particular attribute. Thus by these proofs it is established that it is impossible for His knowledge to be identical to His essence.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration by proofs that God's knowledge cannot be identical to His essence. The author begins by stating that the two possibilities (knowledge being identical to essence or other than it) are both false, then focuses on the first. He presents five arguments. (1) A simple statement of existence conveys no additional meaning, while 'Knowing' adds meaning, so they must be distinct. (2) The contradictions of the two statements are different, and non-contradiction between them shows conceptual distinctness. (3) A proposition with distinct subject and predicate can be doubted, but if they were identical the proposition would be trivial. (4) We can conceive of one without the other, which implies distinctness. (5) Knowledge is a relation, which differs from the self-subsistent essence. Hence identity is impossible.

Key Terms
عين الذات
تغاير
واجب الوجود
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge is simply the same as His essence, making the statement 'God is knowing' redundant.
Correction: The first proof shows that the statement adds meaning, so the concepts are not identical.

Checkpoint

A) The statement 'Necessary Existence is knowing' is meaningless.
B) The statement 'Necessary Existence is knowing' adds meaning not present in 'Necessary Existence' alone.
C) The contradictions are the same.
D) The essence is simple.

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs establish that God's knowledge and essence are distinct through additional meaning and different contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knowing,' do you simply recite it as a name, or do you truly feel that this attribute adds a distinct perfection to His essence? How will you train your heart to appreciate the addition of each attribute in your supplications?
?
The proof shows that denying knowledge is not the same as denying existence. Have you ever treated Allah's attributes as interchangeable, losing the specific awe due to each? What one attribute will you focus on this week to worship Him with greater awareness?
?
If someone asked you tomorrow why God's knowledge is not just His essence, would you be able to explain using the argument of contradiction? Commit today to memorizing this proof so you can defend the beauty of Allah's attributes.

The Remaining Three Proofs That Knowledge Is Not Identical to Essence
The third, fourth, and fifth proofs against identity of knowledge and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third, fourth, and fifth proofs show that God's knowledge cannot be identical to His essence through propositional intelligibility, conceivability of separation, and the nature of knowledge as a relation.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a relation, separable in conception, and distinct from the self-subsistent essence.

After the first two linguistic and logical proofs, the author presents three more proofs that reinforce the distinctness of God's knowledge and essence. The third proof considers the proposition 'Necessary Existence is knowing' as meaningful and subject to doubt, which requires distinct subject and predicate. The fourth proof uses the fact that we can conceive of one without the other. The fifth proof defines knowledge as a relation, which is inherently distinct from the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Examine the third proof
Read the third proof carefully. The statement 'Necessary Existence is knowing' is a meaningful proposition that can be doubted. This would not be possible if the predicate were identical to the subject.
Note the phrase 'قابلة للتصديق والتكذيب' — capable of being affirmed or denied.
2
Reflect on the fourth proof
The fourth proof relies on our ability to conceive of Necessary Existence without knowledge and knowledge without Necessary Existence. If they were identical, conceiving one would entail the other.
The argument uses the principle: if two concepts are distinct, one can be conceived without the other.
3
Understand the fifth proof
The fifth proof defines knowledge as a 'particular relation' (تعلق مخصوص) or 'particular attribute' (نسبة مخصوصة), while the essence is 'self-subsistent' (ذات قائمة بنفسها). A relation is ontologically dependent on its relata and thus distinct from a self-subsistent entity.
This is a metaphysical argument based on the nature of attributes and essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون عين ذاته، وإما أن يكون غير ذاته، والقسمان باطلان فبطل الملزوم. أما الحصر فظاهر. وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون علمه بالمعلومات عين ذاته فلوجوه: الأول: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن قولنا: واجب الوجود فإنه لا يفيد شيئاً. وقولنا: واجب الوجود عالم يفيد معنى منظوماً، ولولا التغاير لما حصل الفرق. الثاني: إن نقيض قولنا: إنه واجب الوجود لذاته هو أنه ليس واجب الوجود. ونقيض قولنا: إنه عالم هو أنه ليس بعالم. ومعلوم بالضرورة أن قولنا: إنه عالم لا يناقضه قولنا إنه ليس واجب الوجود. وقولنا: إنه واجب الوجود لا يناقضه قولنا: إنه ليس بعالم، ولولا أن المفهوم من قولنا: إنه عالم ليس نفس المفهوم من قولنا: إنه واجب الوجود لذاته لما صحت هذه الأحكام. الثالث: إنا إذا قلنا: واجب الوجود عالم. فهذه قضية مفهومة، قابلة للتصديق والتكذيب في الشك والشبهة. وكل قضية يكون محمولها غير موضوعها، فإنها لا تكون كذلك. الرابع: وهو أنه يمكننا تعقل كونه واجب الوجود لذاته مع الشك في كونه عالماً، ويمكننا تعقل كونه عالماً مع الشك في كونه واجب الوجود لذاته. وذلك يوجب التغاير فإن المعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم. الخامس: أنا بينا أن العلم عبارة عن التعلق المخصوص وعن النسبة المخصوصة، وذاته تعالى ذات قائمة بنفسها، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن الذات القائمة بنفسها مغايرة للنسبة المخصوصة، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه يمتنع أن يكون علمه عين ذاته.
It would be identical to His essence, or it would be other than His essence, and both divisions are false, so the underlying premise is false. As for the exhaustive division, it is obvious. As for that it is impossible for His knowledge of things to be identical to His essence, there are proofs: First: We know necessarily that our statement 'Necessary Existence' does not convey any meaning [beyond existence], and our statement 'Necessary Existence is knowing' conveys an additional meaning, and were it not for distinctness, no difference would arise. Second: The contradictory of our statement 'He is Necessary Existence in Himself' is 'He is not Necessary Existence,' and the contradictory of our statement 'He is knowing' is 'He is not knowing.' And it is known necessarily that our statement 'He is knowing' does not contradict our statement 'He is not Necessary Existence,' and our statement 'He is Necessary Existence' does not contradict our statement 'He is not knowing.' And were it not that the concept from our statement 'He is knowing' is not the same concept from our statement 'He is Necessary Existence in Himself,' these judgments would not be correct. Third: If we say 'Necessary Existence is knowing,' this is an intelligible proposition, capable of being affirmed or denied in doubt and ambiguity. Any proposition whose predicate is not identical to its subject is not [capable of this]. Fourth: It is possible for us to conceive Him as Necessary Existence in Himself while doubting that He is knowing, and it is possible for us to conceive Him as knowing while doubting that He is Necessary Existence in Himself. This necessitates distinctness, for the known is distinct from that which is not known. Fifth: We have shown that knowledge is an expression of a particular relation and a particular attribute, and His exalted essence is an essence subsistent by itself, and it is known necessarily that the essence subsistent by itself is distinct from the particular attribute. Thus by these proofs it is established that it is impossible for His knowledge to be identical to His essence.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration by proofs that God's knowledge cannot be identical to His essence. The author begins by stating that the two possibilities (knowledge being identical to essence or other than it) are both false, then focuses on the first. He presents five arguments. (1) A simple statement of existence conveys no additional meaning, while 'Knowing' adds meaning, so they must be distinct. (2) The contradictions of the two statements are different, and non-contradiction between them shows conceptual distinctness. (3) A proposition with distinct subject and predicate can be doubted, but if they were identical the proposition would be trivial. (4) We can conceive of one without the other, which implies distinctness. (5) Knowledge is a relation, which differs from the self-subsistent essence. Hence identity is impossible.

تعلق
نسبة
عين الذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because God is simple, His knowledge must be identical to His essence.
Correction: These proofs show that simplicity does not entail identity; distinct concepts can coexist without composition.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof use to show that knowledge is distinct from essence?
A) Knowledge is an action.
B) Knowledge is a relation, while essence is self-subsistent.
C) Knowledge is eternal.
D) Knowledge is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The third, fourth, and fifth proofs reinforce that God's knowledge is distinct from His essence through propositional meaning, separate conceivability, and the relational nature of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rely on God's knowledge of your every circumstance, or do you sometimes doubt because you subtly equate His knowledge with His essence, as if He must act a certain way? How does the distinctness of His knowledge from His essence affect your trust in His wisdom?
?

?
In which area of your life do you treat God's attribute of knowledge as identical to His essence—meaning you assume He knows but fail to see the relational aspect that requires you to seek His guidance actively? What will you change today to align your actions with the truth that His knowledge is a relation that engages with your specific needs?

Proof That God's Knowledge Is Not Separate from His Essence
The author presents a proof that God's knowledge cannot be separate from His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that God's knowledge, being an attribute dependent on the essence, must be identical with the essence because it requires an efficient cause which is only the essence itself.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be separate because it would need a cause—and only the essence can be that cause.

This passage presents a foundational argument in divine attributes: that God's knowledge cannot be a separate entity from His essence. The author uses a dichotomy: either knowledge is self-subsistent or it is an attribute dependent on the essence. By eliminating the first option, he establishes that knowledge is contingent and requires an efficient cause, which must be the essence itself. This reading will walk you through the logical steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the clear structure: a claim, a dichotomy, rejection of one option, and a conclusion.
The proof is concise; each sentence is a logical step.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author divides knowledge into two: either self-subsistent or an attribute subsistent by the essence. Mark these two and understand why the first is rejected.
The rejection is because attributes cannot be self-subsistent.
3
Trace the causal argument
Once knowledge is an attribute dependent on essence, it is contingent and needs an efficient cause. See how the author concludes that cause is only the essence.
This uses the principle that every contingent needs a cause.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof implies that knowledge cannot be separate: if it were separate, it would need a cause outside the essence, but that is impossible.
The proof aims to show identity between knowledge and essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون علمه غير ذاته. فالدليل عليه: أن ذلك العلم إما أن يكون موجوداً قائماً بنفسه، وإما أن يكون صفة قائمة بذاته، والأول باطل، لأن كونه عالماً بالمعلوم صفة له، وصفة الشيء يمتنع كونها قائمة بنفسها، فيثبت أن كونه عالماً بالأشياء صفة مفتقرة إلى ذاته، والصفة المفتقرة إلى الذات تكون ممكنة بنفسها، وكل ممكن فلا بد له من مؤثر، وذلك المؤثر ليس إلا تلك الذات. إلا أن هذا باطل من وجوه: الأول: إن ذاته مؤثرة في وجود العالم، فلو كانت مؤثرة في حصول ذلك العلم، لكان قد صدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد، وهو محال. والثاني: إن الموصوف بذلك العلم هو تلك الذات، فلو كان المؤثر فيه أيضاً نفس تلك الذات لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً، وهو محال. والثالث: إن افتقار الأثر إلى المؤثر يمتنع أن يحصل حال بقاء الأثر، وإلا لزم تكوين الكائن وتحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال. فوجب أن يكون افتقاره إليه حال العدم أو حال الحدوث. وعلى التقديرين، فكل ما يكون مفتقراً إلى المؤثر فإنه محدث. فوجب في ما لا يكون محدثاً أن لا يكون مفتقراً إلى المؤثر، وعالمية الله قديمة، فامتنع افتقارها إلى المؤثر.
As for the demonstration that it is impossible for His knowledge to be other than His essence, the proof for it is: That knowledge is either an existence subsistent by itself, or an attribute subsistent by the essence. The first is invalid, because His being knowing of the known is an attribute of Him, and it is impossible for an attribute of a thing to be subsistent by itself. So it is established that His being knowing of things is an attribute dependent upon His essence. An attribute dependent upon the essence is contingent in itself, and every contingent must have an efficient cause, and that efficient cause is only that essence. Except that this [proof] is invalid from several aspects: First: His essence is effective in the existence of the world, so if it were effective in bringing about that knowledge, then more than one would have proceeded from the One, which is impossible. Second: The one described by that knowledge is that essence, so if the efficient cause of it were also that same essence, then one and the same thing would be, with respect to one and the same thing, both receptive and agent simultaneously, which is impossible. Third: The dependence of the effect upon the efficient cause cannot occur during the persistence of the effect, otherwise that would entail the bringing into being of what already is and the obtaining of what is already obtained, which is impossible. So its dependence upon it must be during non-existence or during origination. In either case, everything that is dependent upon an efficient cause is originated. So it is necessary for what is not originated not to be dependent upon an efficient cause, and God's knowledge is eternal, so its dependence upon an efficient cause is impossible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God's knowledge cannot be other than His essence, then refutes that proof through three objections. The proof begins by dividing the knowledge into either self-subsistent or an attribute dependent on essence. The first is rejected because attributes are not self-subsistent. Thus knowledge is an attribute dependent on essence, which is contingent and requires an efficient cause, which must be the essence itself. However, the author then shows the proof is invalid. First, if the essence causes both the world and knowledge, then two effects proceed from one cause, violating the principle 'from one only one proceeds.' Second, the same entity would be both the agent and the recipient of the attribute, which is impossible. Third, dependence on an efficient cause is only during non-existence or origination, but God's knowledge is eternal, so it cannot depend on a cause. Thus the original proof fails, and the conclusion is that knowledge cannot be separate because it would entail impossible consequences.

غير الذات
صفة قائمة
ممكن
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if knowledge is an attribute, it could be a separate entity that Allah possesses, like a tool.
Correction: The proof shows that an attribute cannot be self-subsistent; it must inhere in an essence. Therefore, knowledge cannot be a separate entity; it is ontologically dependent on the essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that God's knowledge is a self-subsistent existence?
A) Because knowledge is not an attribute.
B) Because an attribute of a thing cannot be self-subsistent.
C) Because self-subsistent things are impossible.
D) Because the essence is the only self-subsistent.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge cannot be separate from His essence because it is a contingent attribute that requires a cause, and that cause can only be the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is not separate from His essence, then His knowledge is His very being. How does this truth challenge your tendency to compartmentalize God's attributes or think of Him in parts? Will you strive to worship God as a unified, simple Being whose knowledge is inseparable from His essence?
?
The proof shows that every contingent attribute depends on its cause. Reflect on your own contingent existence and attributes. What are you relying on as the ultimate cause of your being? Is it truly God alone, or are you attributing causal power to created things?
?
Consider the implication that God's knowledge is caused by His essence, yet that causation is eternal. How does this reshape your understanding of divine simplicity? Are you willing to accept that God's attributes are not added to His essence but are aspects of a single reality, even if it challenges your ordinary way of thinking?

Three Objections to the Proof of Knowledge as Separate
The author presents three objections refuting the proof that knowledge is caused by the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections that refute the proof that God's knowledge must be caused by His essence, and how they lead to the conclusion that knowledge is eternal and uncaused.

Core Idea
If knowledge were caused by the essence, it would violate principles of divine unity and causality.

After presenting a proof that God's knowledge must be caused by His essence, the author turns around and refutes it with three powerful objections. These objections draw on well-known kalam principles: that from one only one can proceed, that the same thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and that dependence on a cause implies temporality. By showing that these principles contradict the proof's conclusion, the author defends the orthodox view that God's knowledge is eternal and identical with His essence, not a caused attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and conclusion
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three objections are numbered, followed by a concluding argument based on the third objection.
The objections are independent but all target the idea of knowledge being caused by the essence.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection uses the principle 'from one only one proceeds' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه أكثر من واحد). Since the essence already causes the world, it cannot also cause knowledge.
This challenges the possibility of multiple effects from a single cause.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection states that the same thing cannot be both agent and patient (قابل وفاعل) relative to the same thing. Here, the essence would be both the subject that knows and the cause of that knowledge.
This is a logical impossibility.
4
Understand the third objection and its conclusion
The third objection says that dependence on a cause only applies during non-existence or origination, not after existence. Since God's knowledge is eternal, it cannot be dependent on a cause, so it cannot be separate.
This objection leads to the final conclusion that knowledge is uncaused and eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the demonstration that it is impossible for His knowledge to be other than His essence, the proof for it is: That knowledge is either an existence subsistent by itself, or an attribute subsistent by the essence. The first is invalid, because His being knowing of the known is an attribute of Him, and it is impossible for an attribute of a thing to be subsistent by itself. So it is established that His being knowing of things is an attribute dependent upon His essence. An attribute dependent upon the essence is contingent in itself, and every contingent must have an efficient cause, and that efficient cause is only that essence. Except that this [proof] is invalid from several aspects: First: His essence is effective in the existence of the world, so if it were effective in bringing about that knowledge, then more than one would have proceeded from the One, which is impossible. Second: The one described by that knowledge is that essence, so if the efficient cause of it were also that same essence, then one and the same thing would be, with respect to one and the same thing, both receptive and agent simultaneously, which is impossible. Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God's knowledge cannot be other than His essence, then refutes that proof through three objections. The proof begins by dividing the knowledge into either self-subsistent or an attribute dependent on essence. The first is rejected because attributes are not self-subsistent. Thus the original proof fails, and the conclusion is that knowledge cannot be separate because it would entail impossible consequences.

?

But your own knowledge and understanding are created and limited. How often do you act as if your finite perspective is self-sufficient? In what specific area of your life are you refusing to acknowledge that your knowledge is dependent on His will and guidance?
?

But reflect on your own spiritual life: Are you trying to be both the cause and the receiver of your own growth—earning salvation through deeds while also claiming reliance on grace? How can you shift from being the agent of your own righteousness to being a pure recipient of His mercy?

Refuting the Claim That God's Knowledge Is Other Than His Essence
Razi presents a proof and its refutation with three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi demonstrates that the proof that God's knowledge must be other than His essence is invalid, using three kalām objections.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge cannot be dependent on an external cause without implying impossibility.

In this passage, al-Razi first presents a philosophical proof that God's knowledge must be an attribute distinct from His essence because it depends on the essence as its effecter. Then he refutes that proof with three powerful objections drawn from the principles of kalām. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping the classical Ashʿarī position that divine attributes are neither identical to nor separate from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof for knowledge being other than essence
Read the first part of the Arabic passage carefully. It argues that knowledge cannot be self-subsistent, so it must be an attribute dependent on essence, requiring an effecter (the essence itself).
2
Identify the three objections
Al-Razi then refutes this proof with three distinct objections. Find each one and understand the logical problem it raises.
The first objection concerns the principle 'from the One only one proceeds'; the second concerns the impossibility of the same thing being both receptive and active; the third concerns dependence implying temporality.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: What do these objections tell us about the nature of divine attributes? Why is it impossible for God's knowledge to be 'other than His essence' in the sense implied by the proof?
The refutation shows that the proof fails because it leads to absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون علمه غير ذاته. فالدليل عليه : أن ذلك العلم إما أن يكون موجوداً قائماً بنفسه، وإما أن يكون صفة قائمة بذاته ، والأول باطل ، لأن كونه عالماً بالمعلوم صفة له ، وصفة الشيء يمتنع كونه قائماً بذاته ، فيثبت أن كونه عالماً بالأشياء صفة مفتقرة إلى ذاته ، والصفة المفتقرة إلى الذات تكون ممكنة بنفسها ، وكل ممكن فلا بد له من مؤثر ، وذلك المؤثر ليس إلا تلك الذات
إلا أن هذا باطل من وجوه :
الأول : إن ذاته مؤثرة في وجود العالم ، فلو كانت مؤثرة في حصول ذلك العلم ، لكان قد صدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد ، وهو محال
والثاني : إن الموصوف بذلك العلم ، هو تلك الذات ، فلو كان المؤثر فيه أيضاً نفس تلك الذات لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً ، وهو محال
والثالث : إن افتقار الأثر إلى المؤثر ، يمتنع أن يحصل حال بقاء الأثر ، وإلا لزم تكوين الكائن ، وتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو محال . فوجب أن يكون افتقاره إليه حال العدم أو حال الحدوث. وعلى التقديرين ، فكل ما يكون مفتقراً إلى المؤثر ، فإنه محدث. فوجب إذا لا يكون محدثاً أن لا يكون مفتقراً إلى المؤثر ، وعالمية الله قديمة ، فامتنع افتقارها إلى المؤثر
And as for the demonstration that it is impossible for His knowledge to be other than His essence, the proof for it is: that knowledge is either an entity subsisting by itself or an attribute subsisting in a subject. The first is invalid because His being knowing of the known is an attribute of Him, and an attribute of a thing cannot subsist by itself. Thus it is established that His being knowing of things is an attribute dependent on His essence. An attribute dependent on the essence is contingent in itself, and every contingent necessarily has an effecter, and that effecter is none other than that essence. However, this is invalid from several aspects: First: His essence is the effecter in the existence of the world; if it were the effecter in the occurrence of that knowledge, then more than one would proceed from the One, which is impossible. Second: The subject qualified by that knowledge is that same essence; if the effecter of it were also that same essence, then the one thing with respect to the one thing would be both receptive and active together, which is impossible. Third: The dependence of the effect on the effecter cannot occur while the effect endures, otherwise it would entail the creation of the created and the obtaining of the obtained, which is impossible. So its dependence on the effecter must be either in the state of non-existence or in the state of origination. In both cases, everything that depends on an effecter is originated. Therefore, whatever is not originated must not depend on an effecter. And God's omniscience is eternal, so its dependence on an effecter is impossible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument about divine attributes. It begins by stating a proof that God's knowledge cannot be other than His essence. The proof is that knowledge must be either a self-subsistent entity or an attribute inhering in a subject. It cannot be self-subsistent because knowledge is an attribute of the knower. So it must be an attribute dependent on the essence. Any dependent attribute is contingent and requires an effecter, which can only be the essence itself. The author then refutes this proof with three objections. The first objection: if the essence were the effecter of knowledge, then from the one God there would proceed both the world and knowledge, which violates the principle that from the One only one thing can proceed. The second objection: the essence would be both the subject and the effecter of knowledge, making it simultaneously receptive and active, which is impossible. The third objection: dependence on an effecter is only possible at the moment of origination, but since God's knowledge is eternal and not originated, it cannot depend on an effecter. Thus the proof that knowledge must be other than the essence is refuted.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
ذات (essence)
صفة (attribute)
مؤثر (effecter)
قديم (eternal)
محدث (originated)
واحد (One)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against the proof that God's knowledge must be other than His essence?
A) Knowledge cannot be an attribute.
B) The essence would cause both the world and knowledge, violating the principle that from the One only one proceeds.
C) Knowledge is eternal and cannot have a cause.
D) The essence is simple and cannot be an effecter.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the claim that divine knowledge must be other than essence by showing that the proof for it leads to three impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just studied a proof that attempts to show God's knowledge must be separate from His essence, and al-Razi's refutation based on logical impossibilities. How does this affect your certainty that God's knowledge of you is not caused by your actions, but is eternal and unchanging? Are there moments when you live as if God's knowledge of your future is uncertain? What would change if you truly believed that His knowledge of every detail of your life is timeless and uncaused?
?
Consider the objection that if God's essence were both the subject and the cause of knowledge, it would be contradictory. Does this deepen your appreciation for God's transcendence and His being beyond human categories? In what area of your life do you need to submit to the reality that God's attributes are beyond your full comprehension? What is one specific way you can worship Him with that understanding today?
?
This passage emphasizes that only originated things depend on an effecter, but God's knowledge is eternal. How does this confront any tendency you have to think that God's knowledge could be 'added to' or 'diminished' by your deeds? What is the practical consequence of knowing that nothing you do changes God's perfect knowledge of you?

The Second Doubt: Knowledge as a Perfection Attribute
Razi presents a dilemma: either knowledge is a perfection or not, both leading to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Razi uses to challenge the view that divine knowledge is a distinct attribute added to God's essence.

Core Idea
If knowledge perfects God, His essence is deficient; if not, He cannot have it.

This lesson examines a powerful doubt raised by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. He forces a choice between two unacceptable alternatives: either knowledge is a perfection attribute, implying God's essence is deficient without it, or it is not a perfection, implying God cannot be described by it. We will unpack this dilemma and see how it challenges the notion of distinct divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Notice the structure of the dilemma: two clear cases introduced by 'إما' and 'وإما'.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Understand the first case: if knowledge is a perfection, then God's essence would need it. The second case: if it is not a perfection, God cannot possess it.
Each horn leads to an impossibility.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this dilemma is problematic for those who claim knowledge is a distinct attribute of God. What does it suggest about the nature of divine attributes?
This supports the doctrine that God's attributes are identical to His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أن يقال : العلم إما أن يكون صفة كمال ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول فيلزم أن يقال : إن ذاته ناقصة بذاتها ، مستكملة بتلك الصفة المغايرة ، والناقص يمتنع كونه إلهاً ، وإن كان الثاني وجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه ، لأن ما لا يكون صفة كمال ، امتنع الحكم بكونه تعالى موصوفاً به
The second doubt: It may be said: Knowledge is either a perfection attribute or not. If the first, then it follows that His essence is deficient in itself, being perfected by that distinct attribute, and the deficient cannot be God. If the second, then God must be declared transcendent of it, because that which is not a perfection attribute cannot be judged as an attribute of Him.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection (shubha) concerning the nature of divine knowledge. It presents a dilemma: either knowledge is a perfection attribute or it is not. If knowledge is a perfection, then God's essence would be deficient on its own and would need that attribute to become perfect, which is impossible for God. If knowledge is not a perfection, then God cannot be described by it, because He is only described by perfect attributes. Thus, in either case, the claim that God has knowledge distinct from His essence leads to a contradiction. This objection is used to argue that God's knowledge must be identical to His essence.

Key Terms
صفة كمال (perfection attribute)
ناقص (deficient)
تنزيه (transcendence)
إله (God)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge can be a separate perfection attribute added to God's essence.
Correction: The dilemma shows that whether knowledge is considered a perfection or not, it cannot be a distinct attribute; both options lead to contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does the second doubt conclude about the nature of divine knowledge?
A) Knowledge must be a perfection attribute.
B) Knowledge cannot be a separate attribute from essence.
C) Knowledge is neither a perfection nor imperfection.
D) Knowledge is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The second doubt demonstrates that the notion of a distinct attribute of knowledge leads to an impossibility, thereby supporting the view that God's knowledge is identical to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you truly accept that God is absolutely perfect without needing anything, or do you sometimes act as if your worship adds something to Him? How does this dilemma expose a hidden belief that God might be deficient without your obedience?
?
The dilemma forces a logical contradiction if attributes are distinct. Do you feel unsettled by this? How does it push you to rely on revelation while affirming that God's knowledge is not separate from His essence? What change in your heart is required to fully trust this?

The Third Doubt: Knowledge as Relation Implies Contingency
Razi presents an objection that knowledge as relation leads to contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if divine knowledge is a relation, then God's essence depends on something external, making the Necessary Existent contingent.

Core Idea
Relation requires two relata; God's knowledge as relation makes Him dependent on creation.

This lesson examines Razi's third doubt against the view that divine knowledge is a relation. He argues that a relation needs two relata, so if knowledge is a relation, God's essence must depend on the known object, implying contingency—contradicting God's necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of relation
Learn that Razi defines knowledge as a specific relation (nisa) that requires two relata: the knower and the known.
The example 'father of Zayd' clarifies that a relation exists only when both terms exist.
2
Follow the logical implication
If God's essence cannot lack knowledge, and knowledge depends on a second relatum (the known), then God's essence depends on something else for its actualization.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Dependence on another makes God contingent, but He is the Necessary Existent—this is a contradiction; therefore knowledge cannot be a relation.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : إنا قد ذكرنا أن العلم نسبة مخصوصة وإضافة مخصوصة وحصول النسبة والإضافة مشروط بحصول المضافين ، مثلا أبوه زيد لعمرو ، لا يمكن حصولها إلا بعد حصول ذات زيد وعمرو ، ولما كانت المعرفة والعلم نسبة مخصوصة وجب أن يكون حصولها موقوفاً على حصول المضافين. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ذات الباري تعالى لا تكون كافية في حصول الصفة المسماة بالعلم ، لأنا بينا : أن العلم نسبة، وحصول النسبة لا يستقل به شيء واحد البتة ، فإذا ذات الباري يمتنع خلوه عن العلم - والعلم موقوف على المضاف الثاني - لزم أن تكون ذات الله تعالى موقوف التحقق على اعتبار غيره ، والموقوف على الغير ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وهو محال
الشبهة الرابعة : قالوا : العلم عند الفلاسفة عبارة عن صورة مطابقة للمعلوم وعند المتكلمين عبارة عن نسبة مخصوصة تحصل بين العلم وبين المعلوم. وعلى كل التقديرات ، فإنه يجب أن يكون العلم بكل معلوم مغاير للعلم بالمعلوم الآخر .
أما على قول الفلاسفة . فلان كل شيء فإنه مخالف للشيء الآخر ، إما بحسب أصل ماهيته وإما بحسب تعينه ، فالصورة المطابقة لأحدهما لا بد وأن تكون مخالفة للصورة المطابقة للأخرى .
وأما على قول المتكلمين : فلان الانتساب إلى هذا يصح تعقله حال الذهول عن الانتساب إلى غيره ، والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم ، فيثبت أن على
The third doubt: We have already mentioned that knowledge is a specific relation and a specific addition. The occurrence of relation and addition is conditioned by the existence of the two relata. For example, 'father of Zayd' for 'Amr' cannot occur except after the existence of the essence of Zayd and 'Amr. Since knowledge and cognition are specific relations, their occurrence must be dependent on the existence of the relata. Having established this, we say: The essence of the Most High is not sufficient for the occurrence of the attribute called knowledge, because we have shown that knowledge is a relation, and no single thing can be independent in the occurrence of a relation. Thus, if the essence of the Most High cannot be devoid of knowledge—and knowledge is dependent on the second relatum—then it follows that the essence of God is dependent for its actualization on the consideration of something other than it. What is dependent on another is contingent in itself, so it follows that the Necessary Existence in itself would be contingent in itself, which is impossible. The fourth doubt: They said: Knowledge, according to the philosophers, is a form that corresponds to the known, and according to the mutakallimun, it is a specific relation that occurs between knowledge and the known. In all cases, it is necessary that knowledge of every known is different from knowledge of another known. According to the philosophers: because every thing is different from another thing, either in the essence of its quiddity or in its individuation, so the form corresponding to one must be different from the form corresponding to the other. According to the mutakallimun: because the relation to this one can be conceived while disregarding the relation to the other, and the known is different from the other known, so it is established that...
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage contains two objections (shubhat) against the identity of divine knowledge with essence. The third doubt argues that if knowledge is a relation (nisa), then its occurrence requires two relata: the knower and the known. Since God cannot be devoid of knowledge, and knowledge depends on an external known, God's essence would depend on something else for its actualization. This would make the Necessary Existent contingent, which is impossible. The fourth doubt, though incomplete, argues that knowledge must differ for each known object. According to philosophers, knowledge is a form corresponding to the known; different knowns require different forms. According to mutakallimun, knowledge is a relation; the relation to one known is distinct from the relation to another. Thus, if God knows multiple objects, He must have multiple knowledges, which contradicts the simplicity of His essence. The passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
نسبة (relation)
إضافة (addition)
مضافين (two relata)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
ممكن (contingent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge as a relation is a common definition among mutakallimun; some may think it is harmless.
Correction: Razi shows it leads to a logical contradiction: if knowledge is a relation, God's essence depends on something external, implying contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the third doubt, why does defining knowledge as a relation lead to a contradiction?
A) Relations are impossible in God.
B) It makes God's knowledge multiple.
C) It makes God's essence depend on external things, implying contingency.
D) It implies that God is ignorant of some things.

Key Takeaway
If divine knowledge is a relation, God would depend on creation, contradicting His necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's knowledge of you does not depend on your existence, how should that reshape your fear of being forgotten or unnoticed by Him?
?
Razi argues that making God's knowledge a relation reduces Him to contingency. In your own thoughts, do you ever unconsciously imagine that God 'needs' your worship or obedience to know you? What does this lesson demand you change?
?
Since divine knowledge must be identical with God's essence, His knowledge of your sins does not cause Him to 'react' as a relation would. How does this truth challenge you to stop projecting human relational patterns onto God and instead trust His fixed, essential knowledge in your repentance?

The Fourth Doubt: Knowledge Multiplicity Begins
Razi begins an argument that knowledge multiplicity follows from both major definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's opening argument that knowledge of each distinct known must be distinct, leading to multiplicity in God's attributes.

Core Idea
Different knowns require different knowledges, threatening divine simplicity.

This lesson presents the beginning of Razi's fourth doubt, where he argues that whether knowledge is a form (philosophers) or a relation (mutakallimun), knowledge of one known is different from knowledge of another. This implies multiplicity in God's knowledge, which conflicts with His simple essence.

Guided Path
1
Two definitions of knowledge
Note that philosophers define knowledge as a corresponding form, while mutakallimun define it as a relation. Razi uses both to argue for multiplicity.
2
Philosophers' perspective
If knowledge is a form that matches the known, then different knowns require different forms, so God's knowledge of each object is distinct.
3
Mutakallimun's perspective
If knowledge is a relation, the relation to one known is conceived apart from another, so they are distinct relations.
The passage cuts off here; the conclusion would be that God would have multiple knowledges.

Source Text

?

?

Can you accept that your own mental categories (form, relation) may be insufficient to describe God? Or will you cling to them, risking a diminished understanding of His unity?

The Third Doubt: Knowledge as a Relation Implies Contingency
The third doubt argues that if knowledge is a relation, God's essence would depend on another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt against the view that God's knowledge is a relation, and why it would lead to the conclusion that God is contingent.

Core Idea
If knowledge is a relation, God's essence would depend on creation.

This lesson presents the third philosophical doubt (shubha) against divine knowledge. The doubter argues that if knowledge is a relation between knower and known, then God's knowledge would require the existence of the known objects, making God's essence dependent on them. This would imply contingency, contradicting God's necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional chain.
The passage is a single continuous argument, not an enumeration.
2
Identify the key assumption
The doubter assumes that knowledge is a 'specific relation' (nisba makhsusa). Understand why this assumption is crucial for the argument.
Relational entities depend on both relata for their existence.
3
Follow the logical consequence
Trace the argument: God has knowledge → knowledge is a relation → relation requires two relata → the second relatum is the known object → so God's essence depends on that object → thus God would be contingent → contradiction.
The conclusion is that if knowledge is a relation, then the Necessary Being would be possible, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on the doubt's purpose
This doubt is not proving that God lacks knowledge; it is showing the consequences of defining knowledge as a relation. Think about how a theologian might respond.
The response would typically deny that knowledge is merely a relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : إنا قد ذكرنا أن العلم نسبة مخصوصة وإضافة مخصوصة وحصول النسبة والإضافة مشروط بحصول المضافين، مثلا أبوه زيد لعمرو، لا يمكن حصولها إلا بعد حصول ذات زيد وعمرو، ولما كانت المعرفة والعلم نسبة مخصوصة وجب أن يكون حصولها موقوفاً على حصول المضافين. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ذات الباري تعالى لا تكون كافية في حصول الصفة المسماة بالعلم، لأنا بينا : أن العلم نسبة، وحصول النسبة لا يستقل به شيء واحد البتة، فإذا ذات الباري يمتنع خلوه عن العلم - والعلم موقوف على المضاف الثاني - لزم أن تكون ذات الله تعالى موقوف التحقق على اعتبار غيره، والموقوف على الغير ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهو محال
The third doubt: We have mentioned that knowledge is a specific relation and a specific addition, and the occurrence of the relation and addition is conditioned by the occurrence of the two relata. For example, 'fatherhood of Zayd to Amr' cannot occur except after the existence of the essence of Zayd and Amr. Since recognition and knowledge are a specific relation, it must be that their occurrence is dependent on the occurrence of the relata. If this is established, we say: The essence of the Most High does not suffice for the occurrence of the attribute called knowledge, because we have shown that knowledge is a relation, and the occurrence of a relation is not independent of any one thing at all. So if the essence of the Most High cannot be devoid of knowledge — and knowledge is dependent on the second relatum — then it necessarily follows that the essence of Allah, Most High, is dependent for its realization on considering something other than it. And that which is dependent on another is possible in itself, so it follows that the Necessary of Existence in itself would be possible of existence in itself, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt (shubha) against the view that God has knowledge as an attribute. The doubt begins by assuming that knowledge is a relation (nisba) and an addition (idafa) between the knower and the known. The doubter argues that a relation requires two relata (mutadifayn) and its occurrence depends on both. For example, the fatherhood of Zayd to Amr cannot be conceived unless both Zayd and Amr exist. Similarly, if knowledge is a relation, then the occurrence of knowledge in God would require the existence of the object of knowledge. Since God's essence is never devoid of knowledge, and knowledge is dependent on the second relatum (the known), it follows that God's essence itself would be dependent on something other than it for its realization. That which depends on another is possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi). Thus, the Necessary of Existence in itself (wajib al-wujud bi-dhatihi) would be possible, which is a contradiction. The argument aims to show that if knowledge is defined as a relation, then God cannot have knowledge without becoming contingent. The response to this doubt would typically deny that knowledge is merely a relation or clarify that the relation is not essential to God's essence.

Key Terms
علم
نسبة
إضافة
مضاف
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third doubt proves that God does not have knowledge.
Correction: The doubt only shows that if knowledge is defined as a relation, then God would be contingent. It does not prove that God lacks knowledge; rather, it challenges the relational definition.

Checkpoint
According to the third doubt, if knowledge is a relation, what follows about God's essence?
A) It is necessary
B) It is contingent
C) It is simple
D) It is eternal

Key Takeaway
The third doubt assumes knowledge is a relation and shows that this leads to the absurd conclusion that God is contingent, thereby challenging the relational view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does this reveal about the nature of God's knowledge versus human knowledge? How should this change the way you understand being known by God?
?
If you truly believed that God is absolutely self-sufficient and in no need of His creation, how would it affect your worship? Would you pray out of necessity or out of gratitude? Spend a moment in silence, asking yourself: Do I act as if God needs my obedience?
?
The doubt uses a reductio ad absurdum to expose a flawed assumption. In your own beliefs about God, are there any hidden assumptions that, if followed to their logical conclusion, would contradict God's perfection? What would it cost you to examine and uproot them?
