The Fourth Doubt: Infinite Knowledge Leads to Infinite Attributes and Contingency
The fourth doubt argues that infinite knowledge implies infinite attributes, impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt against infinite divine knowledge, which argues that infinite knowledge would necessitate an infinite multiplicity of attributes, leading to composition and contingency in God.

Core Idea
Infinite divine knowledge would make God composite and contingent—an impossibility.

This lesson presents a sophisticated doubt from Muslim theologians about the implications of God having infinite knowledge. If God knows an infinite number of objects, then He must have an infinite number of distinct knowledges. This leads to an infinite multiplicity in His essence, which is then shown to be impossible because every multiplicity is finite and composition entails contingency. We will also examine an objection and its response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two definitions of knowledge
Read how philosophers and theologians define knowledge—as a corresponding image or a specific relation. Note that both agree knowledge of each object is distinct.
This sets the premise for multiplicity.
2
Follow the premise leading to infinite attributes
If God knows infinitely many objects, then He must have infinitely many distinct knowledges, meaning infinite attributes in His essence.
This is the core of the doubt.
3
Analyze the first proof against infinite multiplicity
Every multiplicity admits increase and decrease; what admits increase and decrease is finite. Therefore, infinite multiplicity is impossible.
This proof relies on a conceptual analysis of multiplicity.
4
Examine the second proof: composition leads to contingency
If God's essence has parts (attributes), He is composite. Every composite depends on its parts, hence is contingent and requires a cause, leading to an infinite regress.
This attacks the independence of God.
5
Consider the objection and the response
The objection suggests multiplicity is only in attributes, not essence. The response distinguishes two views: if God equals attributes+essence, He is composite; if essence alone, attributes are distinct entities still causing multiplicity.
The argument appears cut off; likely concludes that even the second view fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : قالوا : العلم عند الفلاسفة عبارة عن صورة مطابقة للمعلوم وعند المتكلمين عبارة عن نسبة مخصوصة تحصل بين العلم وبين المعلوم. وعلى كل التقديرات، فإنه يجب أن يكون العلم بكل معلوم مغايراً للعلم بالمعلوم الآخر. أما على قول الفلاسفة. فلان كل شيء فإنه مخالف للشيء الآخر، إما بحسب أصل ماهيته وإما بحسب تعينه، فالصورة المطابقة لأحدهما لا بد وأن تكون مخالفة للصورة المطابقة للأخرى. وأما على قول المتكلمين: فلان الانتساب إلى هذا يصح تعقله حال الذهول عن الانتساب إلى غيره، والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فيثبت أن على القول المنقول عن الفلاسفة وعلى القول المنقول عن المتكلمين يجب أن يكون العلم بكل معلوم مغايراً للعلم بالمعلوم الآخر. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان الباري تعالى عالماً بمعلومات لا نهاية لها، لزم أن يحصل في ذاته صفات لا نهاية لها، وأحوال لا نهاية لها وذلك محال لوجهين: الأول : إن كل كثرة حاصلة بها فإنها تقبل الزيادة والنقصان. وكل ما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان، فإنه متناهي ينتج : أن كل كثرة حاصلة فإنها تكون متناهية. ويلزم : أن ما لا يكون متناهياً، فإنه ممتنع الحصول. والثاني : إن إله العالم إذا كان مركباً من الكثرة، لزم كونه ممكن الوجود لذاته، وذلك لأن كل مركب من الأجزاء فإنه يفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وجزء الشيء غيره، فكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن يكون إله العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته، وما كان ممكن الوجود لذاته وجب افتقاره إلى مؤثر آخر فيلزم افتقار الإله إلى إله آخر، وهو محال. ولا يقال : هذه الكثرة واقعة في العلوم، والعلوم صفات، فالكثرة واقعة في الصفات فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذاته منزهة عن الكثرة وهي موجبة لهذه الصفات ؟ لأننا نقول : الإله إما أن يكون عبارة عن مجموع الصفات، أو هو عبارة عن الذات فقط، والصفات خارجة عن مسمى الإله. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مسمى الإله ممكناً محتاجاً. الذات مع
The fourth doubt: They said: Knowledge according to the philosophers is an image that corresponds to the known, and according to the theologians is a specific relation that occurs between knowledge and the known. On both accounts, it is necessary that knowledge of each known is distinct from knowledge of another known. As for the philosophers' view, because everything is different from another thing, either in the essence of its quiddity or in its particularization, so the image corresponding to one must be different from the image corresponding to the other. As for the theologians' view, because the relation to this one can be conceived while ignoring the relation to another, and the known is different from what is not known, so it is established that according to both transmitted views, knowledge of each known must be distinct from knowledge of another known. If this is established, we say: If the Most High were knowing of infinite objects of knowledge, then it would necessarily follow that there would occur in His essence infinite attributes and infinite states, and that is impossible for two reasons: First, every multiplicity that occurs admits increase and decrease. Everything that admits increase and decrease is finite. The conclusion: every multiplicity that occurs is finite. And it follows that what is not finite is impossible to occur. Second, if the God of the world is composed of multiplicity, then He would be possible of existence in itself, because every composite of parts is dependent on each of its parts, and the part of a thing is other than it, so every composite is dependent on another, so it is possible in itself. Then it would follow that the God of the world is possible in itself, and whatever is possible in itself must be dependent on another effector, so the dependence of God on another god would follow, which is absurd. And it cannot be said: This multiplicity is in the knowledges, and knowledges are attributes, so the multiplicity is in the attributes, so why is it not permissible to say that His essence is free of multiplicity and that it necessitates these attributes? Because we say: God is either the totality of the attributes, or He is only the essence, and the attributes are outside the meaning of 'God.' If the first, then it would follow that the referent of 'God' is possible and needy. The essence with
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt against the possibility of God having infinite knowledge. It begins by noting that both philosophers and theologians agree that knowledge of each object of knowledge must be distinct from knowledge of any other object. Therefore, if God knows an infinite number of objects, He would have an infinite number of distinct knowledges (attributes). The doubt then argues that an infinite multiplicity of attributes is impossible for two reasons. The first reason: every multiplicity (kathra) that occurs admits increase and decrease; whatever admits increase and decrease is finite; therefore, every occurrent multiplicity is finite. Hence, an infinite multiplicity cannot occur. The second reason: if the God of the world is composed of multiplicity, He would be composite (murakkab); every composite depends on its parts, and the parts are other than the whole; thus a composite is dependent on another, making it possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi); but a possible being requires an external cause; so God would depend on another god, which is absurd. The passage then preempts an objection: perhaps the multiplicity is only in the attributes (sifat), not in the essence. The response distinguishes two views: if God is defined as the totality of attributes, then He would be composite and contingent; if He is defined as the essence alone, with attributes as distinct entities, then the attributes are not part of the meaning of 'God,' but the argument still holds. The text ends abruptly with 'al-dhat ma'a' (the essence with), likely continuing to conclude that even on the second view, the essence would be associated with multiplicity, leading to the same problem.

Correction: The doubt does not deny divine knowledge; it targets the idea of multiple distinct attributes in the divine essence, arguing that infinite distinct knowledges would make God composite and contingent.

Proof that Infinite Knowledge is Impossible
The author proves that infinite divine knowledge leads to impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite divine knowledge is impossible according to the author's two-part argument.

Core Idea
Infinite multiplicity cannot exist; therefore God's knowledge must be finite.

In this passage, the author builds on the premise that knowledge of each known is distinct. He then argues that if God knew infinite things, His essence would contain infinite attributes, which leads to two contradictions. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Notice the starting point: knowledge of each known entity is distinct from knowledge of another. This is the basis for the argument.
This premise is taken from both philosophers and theologians.
2
Recognize the conditional
The author posits: if God knew infinite things, then infinite attributes would occur in His essence. This is the key claim to be disproven.
3
Examine the first proof
The first reason: any actual multiplicity can vary in size (increase/decrease), which implies it is finite. Therefore, infinite multiplicity cannot actually exist.
This uses a property of multiplicity.
4
Examine the second proof
The second reason: if God were composed of a multiplicity, He would be compound, dependent on parts, hence contingent. But God cannot be contingent, as that would require another god.
This leads to an infinite regress.
5
Draw the conclusion
Both proofs show that infinite attributes are impossible, so the original conditional (infinite knowledge) must be false.
Thus, divine knowledge is not infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول المنقول عن الفلاسفة وعلى القول المنقول عن المتكلمين يجب أن يكون العلم بكل معلوم مغايراً للعلم بالمعلوم الآخر. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو كان الباري تعالى عالماً بمعلومات لا نهاية لها، لزم أن يحصل في ذاته صفات لا نهاية لها، وأحوال لا نهاية لها وذلك محال لوجهين: الأول: إن كل كثرة حاصلة بها فإنها تقبل الزيادة والنقصان. وكل ما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان، فإنه متناهي فينتج: أن كل كثرة حاصلة فإنها تكون متناهية. ويلزم: أن ما لا يكون متناهياً، فإنه ممتنع الحصول. والثاني: إن إله العالم إذا كان مركباً من الكثرة، لزم كونه ممكن الوجود لذاته، وذلك لأن كل مركب من الأجزاء فإنه يفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وجزء الشيء غيره، فكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن يكون إله العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته، وما كان ممكن الوجود لذاته وجب افتقاره إلى مؤثر آخر فيلزم افتقار الإله إلى إله آخر وهو محال.
The statement transmitted from the philosophers and the statement transmitted from the theologians is that knowledge of each known must be distinct from knowledge of another known. If this is established, we say: If the Exalted Creator were knowing infinite known things, it would necessitate that there would occur in His Essence infinite attributes and infinite states, and that is impossible for two reasons: First, every multiplicity that occurs is subject to increase and decrease, and everything that is subject to increase and decrease is finite, so it follows that every multiplicity that occurs is finite. It follows that what is not finite is impossible to occur. Second, if the God of the world were compounded of multiplicity, it would necessitate that He be contingent in essence, because every compound of parts is in need of each of its parts, and the part of a thing is other than it, so every compound is in need of something other than it, so it is contingent in essence. Consequently, the God of the world would be contingent in essence, and whatever is contingent in essence must be in need of another efficient cause, which would necessitate the God's need of another god, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against infinite divine knowledge by demonstrating that it would lead to impossible consequences. It begins by stating a premise: knowledge of each known entity is distinct from knowledge of another. Then it argues: if God knew an infinite number of things, His essence would contain an infinite number of attributes and states. This is impossible for two reasons. First, any actual multiplicity is subject to increase and decrease, and anything subject to increase and decrease is finite; therefore an infinite multiplicity cannot actually exist. Second, if God were composed of a multiplicity, He would be compound, and every compound depends on its parts, hence contingent; but God cannot be contingent as that would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, infinite knowledge is impossible.

The Objection of Multiplicity in Attributes
Author refutes the objection that multiplicity in attributes is compatible with God's oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that multiplicity in attributes does not affect God's oneness fails, and how the term 'God' is analyzed to show that affirming attributes leads to either composition or extraneous additions.

Core Idea
Affirming attributes without composition or addition is impossible; they must be either part of God's essence or extraneous.

This passage presents a key objection regarding multiplicity in divine attributes and the author's incisive response. The objection claims that multiplicity is only in attributes, not the essence, so God's oneness is preserved. The author responds by examining the meaning of the term 'God,' showing that either attributes make God composite or they are extraneous additions that must be negated. This lesson clarifies the logical necessity of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening phrase: 'ولا يقال... فالكثرة واقعة في الصفات فلم لا يجوز...'. The objector argues that multiplicity is in the attributes, not the essence, so God's essence can still be one while He has multiple attributes.
Notice that the objector assumes attributes are distinct from essence.
2
Analyze the response's dilemma
The author presents two possibilities for what 'God' means: either the sum of attributes or the essence alone. For each, he shows a contradiction: composition/contingency or extraneous additions that must be negated.
This is a classic either-or argument (dilemma).
3
Reflect on the implication for tawhid
Realize that the only way to avoid both horns is to deny that attributes are distinct entities. This leads to the Ash'ari position that attributes are neither identical to the essence nor separate, but 'modes' (ahwal) or other nuanced positions.
The passage itself does not give the solution; it only refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال: هذه الكثرة واقعة في العلوم، والعلوم صفات، فالكثرة واقعة في الصفات فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن ذاته منزهة عن الكثرة وهي موجبة لهذه الصفات؟ لأننا نقول: الإله إما أن يكون عبارة عن مجموع الصفات، أو هو عبارة عن الذات فقط، والصفات خارجة عن مسمى الإله. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مسمى الإله ممكناً محتاجاً. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ إله العالم ليس إلا تلك الذات المجردة عن الصفات فهذه الصفات تكون زائدة على الإله مغايرة لها فوجب نفيها.
And it is not said: This multiplicity occurs in the knowledges, and knowledges are attributes, so multiplicity occurs in the attributes; why then is it not permissible to say that His Essence is free of multiplicity yet necessitates these attributes? Because we say: 'God' is either an expression of the sum of the attributes, or an expression of the Essence alone, and the attributes are outside the denotation of 'God.' If the first, then the denotation of 'God' would be contingent and needy. If the second, then the God of the world is nothing but that Essence stripped of attributes, so these attributes are superadded to God and other than Him, so they must be negated.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage handles an objection: the multiplicity is only in the attributes, not the essence, so why can't God's essence be one while necessitating multiple attributes? The author responds by analyzing the term 'God.' If 'God' means the sum of attributes, then God would be compound and contingent. If 'God' means only the essence, then attributes are extraneous additions, which must be negated to preserve God's simplicity. Thus, the objection fails.

Answer to the First Doubt: Refutation of Three Principles
The author refutes the first doubt by citing earlier refutations of its three premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author dismisses the first doubt by referring to earlier refutations of three faulty principles.

Core Idea
A doubt built on false premises collapses when those premises are refuted.

The first doubt rests on three controversial principles: the one only produces one, a thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and the need for a cause requires temporal origination. The author has already refuted these principles in detail, so he simply points that out. This lesson shows how a strong foundation in earlier discussions can dismantle an objection without repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles
Read the Arabic and translation to list the three principles that the doubt is built upon.
These principles were previously discussed and refuted.
2
Understand the author's strategy
Notice that the author does not re-argue but refers to prior refutations. Why is this efficient?
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this approach preserves the integrity of the earlier arguments and avoids redundancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: إنها بناء على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، وعلى أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معاً، وعلى أن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر، إما الحدوث وإما الإمكان، بشرط الحدوث. وهذه الأصول الثلاثة قد سبق القدح فيها على الاستقصاء، فلا فائدة في الإعادة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: أن نقول: ذاته المخصوصة من حيث هي كاملة لعينها ولذاتها، ومن لوازم ذلك الكمال إيجابها لصفة العلم، وعلى هذا التقدير فالشبهة زائلة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة: إنه إن لزم نفي العلم لكون العلم نسبة موقوفة على حصول المضاف الثاني، لزم كونه مؤثراً في وجود العالم، لأن المؤثر أيضاً من باب النسب والإضافات. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة: إن أبعد الأشياء عن طبائع الكثرة: الوحدة، ثم إنها نصف الاثنين، وثلث الثلاثة، وربع الأربعة، وهلم جرا، إلى ما لا نهاية له من النسب. فيثبت: أن كثرة الإضافات والنسب لا يقدح في حصول الوحدة الذاتية. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب والله أعلم.
The answer to the first doubt: It is based on [the principles] that from the one only one proceeds, and that the one thing cannot be both receptive and active simultaneously, and that the cause of need for an efficient cause is either origination or contingency coupled with origination. These three principles have already been fully refuted, so there is no benefit in repeating. The answer to the second doubt: We say: His specific Essence, inasmuch as it is perfect by virtue of itself and its essence, and among the necessities of that perfection is its necessitating the attribute of knowledge; on this supposition, the doubt is removed. The answer to the third doubt: If the negation of knowledge were necessitated because knowledge is a relation dependent on the existence of the second relatum, then it would necessitate Him being an efficient cause in the existence of the world, because the efficient cause also falls under the category of relations and additions. The answer to the fourth doubt: The furthest thing from the natures of multiplicity is unity; then it is half of two, a third of three, a fourth of four, and so on indefinitely of ratios. Thus it is established that the multiplicity of relations and ratios does not detract from the attainment of essential unity. This completes the discussion of this chapter, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage contains the author's responses to four specific doubts (shubah) that were presumably raised earlier. The first doubt is based on three principles (the one produces only one, a thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and the need for a cause is due to origination or contingency under condition of origination); the author says these have already been refuted, so the doubt is invalid. The second doubt is answered by asserting that God's essence is perfect in itself, and from that perfection necessarily follows the attribute of knowledge; thus no multiplicity in essence is required. The third doubt: if one argues that knowledge as a relation cannot be in God because it depends on the object, the author points out that the same would apply to God's being an efficient cause, which is also a relation; if one accepts that, then knowledge can be affirmed similarly. The fourth doubt concerns whether multiple relations compromise divine unity; the author gives an example: unity is the basis for all numerical relations (half, third, etc.) and yet remains one; similarly, multiple relations do not undermine essential unity. Thus the four doubts are refuted.

A) A new proof against the principles
B) The fact that the principles have already been refuted
C) The harmony between reason and revelation
D) The impossibility of multiple relations

Key Takeaway
When an objection rests on previously refuted premises, a simple reference to that refutation suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What are the foundational beliefs in your understanding of God that you have never examined critically? Is it possible that some of them are shaky premises on which you have built your faith? Will you take time this week to trace them back to the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The author found no benefit in repeating refutations already established. Are you constantly seeking new spiritual experiences and knowledge while neglecting to deepen and reaffirm what you already know? What one truth from this lesson will you deliberately rehearse until it becomes unshakable?
?
The first doubt rests on three specific principles. In your life, you may be building doubts upon unchecked assumptions about God's nature. What is one assumption you hold about God that, if false, would collapse a spiritual struggle you face? Are you willing to have it challenged and refuted?

Answer to the Second Doubt: Knowledge from Essential Perfection
The author argues that divine knowledge is necessitated by God's essential perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine knowledge is a necessary concomitant of God's essential perfection, not a separate addition.

Core Idea
God's perfect essence necessitates knowledge; no multiplicity is required.

The second doubt challenges that knowledge would introduce multiplicity in God. The author responds by showing that God's essence is perfect in itself, and from that perfection, knowledge necessarily follows. This lesson explains how the attribute of knowledge arises without compromising unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer
Read the Arabic and translation for the second doubt. Note how the author links perfection to knowledge.
Key phrase: 'من لوازم ذلك الكمال إيجابها لصفة العلم'
2
Understand 'necessary concomitant'
The term 'lazim' (necessary consequence) is important. Knowledge is not an accident but a necessary outcome of perfection.
3
Apply the logic
Consider: If something is perfect by itself, does it need to acquire knowledge from outside? No, its knowledge is intrinsic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: إنها بناء على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، وعلى أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معاً، وعلى أن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر، إما الحدوث وإما الإمكان، بشرط الحدوث. وهذه الأصول الثلاثة قد سبق القدح فيها على الاستقصاء، فلا فائدة في الإعادة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: أن نقول: ذاته المخصوصة من حيث هي كاملة لعينها ولذاتها، ومن لوازم ذلك الكمال إيجابها لصفة العلم، وعلى هذا التقدير فالشبهة زائلة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة: إنه إن لزم نفي العلم لكون العلم نسبة موقوفة على حصول المضاف الثاني، لزم كونه مؤثراً في وجود العالم، لأن المؤثر أيضاً من باب النسب والإضافات. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة: إن أبعد الأشياء عن طبائع الكثرة: الوحدة، ثم إنها نصف الاثنين، وثلث الثلاثة، وربع الأربعة، وهلم جرا، إلى ما لا نهاية له من النسب. فيثبت: أن كثرة الإضافات والنسب لا يقدح في حصول الوحدة الذاتية. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب والله أعلم.
The answer to the first doubt: It is based on [the principles] that from the one only one proceeds, and that the one thing cannot be both receptive and active simultaneously, and that the cause of need for an efficient cause is either origination or contingency coupled with origination. These three principles have already been fully refuted, so there is no benefit in repeating. The answer to the second doubt: We say: His specific Essence, inasmuch as it is perfect by virtue of itself and its essence, and among the necessities of that perfection is its necessitating the attribute of knowledge; on this supposition, the doubt is removed. The answer to the third doubt: If the negation of knowledge were necessitated because knowledge is a relation dependent on the existence of the second relatum, then it would necessitate Him being an efficient cause in the existence of the world, because the efficient cause also falls under the category of relations and additions. The answer to the fourth doubt: The furthest thing from the natures of multiplicity is unity; then it is half of two, a third of three, a fourth of four, and so on indefinitely of ratios. Thus it is established that the multiplicity of relations and ratios does not detract from the attainment of essential unity. This completes the discussion of this chapter, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage contains the author's responses to four specific doubts (shubah) that were presumably raised earlier. The first doubt is based on three principles (the one produces only one, a thing cannot be both agent and recipient, and the need for a cause is due to origination or contingency under condition of origination); the author says these have already been refuted, so the doubt is invalid. The second doubt is answered by asserting that God's essence is perfect in itself, and from that perfection necessarily follows the attribute of knowledge; thus no multiplicity in essence is required. The third doubt: if one argues that knowledge as a relation cannot be in God because it depends on the object, the author points out that the same would apply to God's being an efficient cause, which is also a relation; if one accepts that, then knowledge can be affirmed similarly. The fourth doubt concerns whether multiple relations compromise divine unity; the author gives an example: unity is the basis for all numerical relations (half, third, etc.) and yet remains one; similarly, multiple relations do not undermine essential unity. Thus the four doubts are refuted.

Divine knowledge is not an external attribute but a necessary consequence of God's own perfect essence.

Answer to the Third Doubt: Knowledge and Causality as Relations
The author uses an argument of parity to defend divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the objection to divine knowledge as a relation can be turned against itself by pointing out that efficient causality is also a relation.

Core Idea
If knowledge as a relation is denied, then causality must also be denied, which is absurd.

The third doubt argues that knowledge, being a relation dependent on an object, cannot be in God. The author cleverly responds: if that logic holds, then God's being an efficient cause, also a relation, would likewise be impossible. But since we affirm God as cause, we can affirm knowledge. This lesson explores the power of parity arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt's core
The doubt claims knowledge is a relation requiring a second relatum, thus unsuitable for God.
2
See the author's counter
The author points out that efficient causality is also a relation (min bab al-nisab wa al-idafat).
If one denies knowledge, one must also deny causality.
3
Reflect on the implication
Since we accept God as efficient cause, we must accept knowledge on similar grounds.

Source Text

Answer to the Fourth Doubt: Unity Underlies Multiplicity of Relations
The author uses a mathematical analogy to show that unity can sustain multiple relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that essential unity is not compromised by multiple relations, just as the number one underlies all numerical ratios.

Core Idea
Many relations can coexist with essential unity; unity itself is the basis for multiplicity.

The fourth doubt contends that multiple relations would destroy divine unity. The author provides a mathematical example: the number one is the most remote from multiplicity, yet it is half of two, a third of three, and so on indefinitely. This shows that unity can be the foundation for infinite relations without being divided. This lesson explains the analogy and its theological application.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth doubt. Focus on the numerical example.
2
Understand the example
Unity is half of two, third of three, etc. It remains one while entering into many relations.
The example shows that relations do not fragment the essence.
3
Apply to God
Similarly, God's essential unity is not compromised by the multiplicity of relations He has with creation.

Refuting the First Doubt on Divine Attributes
Author identifies three refuted principles underlying a doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three principles underlying the first doubt about divine attributes and why the author considers the doubt already resolved.

Core Idea
The doubt is only as strong as its three already-refuted principles.

Sanusi now addresses the first of four doubts raised against affirming divine attributes. Rather than constructing a new argument, he shows that the entire doubt rests on three principles that have already been thoroughly refuted earlier in the work. In this lesson, we will identify these three principles and understand why the author confidently dismisses the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three principles the author lists as the basis of the doubt.
The passage is concise; each principle is clearly separated by 'وعلى أن'.
2
Identify the three principles
Extract each of the three principles from the passage. Understand what each means: (1) from one only one proceeds, (2) a single thing cannot be both receptive and active, (3) the cause of need for an effecter is either origination or possibility with condition of origination.
3
Understand the author's response
Note that the author does not refute them again here; he simply states they have already been refuted. This is a common argumentative strategy: dismantling the foundation rather than the conclusion.
This shows the interconnectedness of kalam arguments.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if these three principles are false, what happens to the doubt? This demonstrates the power of foundational refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : إنها بناء على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، وعلى أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معاً . وعلى أن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر ، إما الحدوث وإما الإمكان ، بشرط الحدوث . وهذه الأصول الثلاثة قد سبق القدح فيها على الاستقصاء ، فلا فائدة في الإعادة .
The answer to the first doubt: It is based on that from the one only one proceeds, and that the single thing cannot be both receptive and agent simultaneously, and that the cause of the need for an effecter is either origination or possibility, with the condition of origination. These three principles have already been fully refuted, so it is pointless to repeat.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the first of four doubts raised against the affirmation of divine attributes. The author explains that this doubt is built upon three underlying principles: (1) from the one only one can proceed, (2) a single thing cannot be both receptive and active, and (3) the reason something needs an effecter is either its temporal origination or its ontological possibility conditioned by origination. The author states that these three principles have already been thoroughly refuted earlier in the work, so there is no need to repeat the refutation. Thus, the doubt falls.

Key Terms
شبهة
واحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
قابل وفاعل
علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر
حدوث
إمكان
قدح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is presenting a new refutation in this passage.
Correction: The author is merely referencing earlier refutations; he does not repeat them here.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first doubt, what are the three principles on which the doubt is based?
A) From the one only one proceeds, a single thing can be both receptive and active, and the cause of need is determinism.
B) From the one only one proceeds, a single thing cannot be both receptive and active, and the cause of need for an effecter is either origination or possibility with the condition of origination.
C) From the one many proceed, a single thing can be both receptive and active, and the cause of need is eternal causation.
D) The doubt is not based on any principles.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt collapses because its three foundational principles have already been refuted; the author saves ink by not repeating those refutations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a doubt about God – whether about His attributes, His actions, or His commands – without realizing that the doubt rests on assumptions that have already been refuted? What would it take for you to honestly examine those assumptions and let go of the doubt today?
?
This lesson shows that a doubt collapses once its foundations are shown to be false. Is there a belief you hold that secretly relies on principles you've never questioned? Will you trace it back to its roots and see if those roots are true?
?
The author says it is 'pointless to repeat' refutations. Are you still asking the same questions that have already been answered by scholars before you? What is keeping you from accepting those answers and moving forward in your faith?

From Divine Perfection to Knowledge
The author answers the second doubt by affirming that essence necessitates knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfect essence of God necessarily entails the attribute of knowledge, resolving a common doubt about divine attributes.

Core Idea
God's essence is perfect; knowledge necessarily follows from that perfection.

This passage addresses a theological doubt: if knowledge is a distinct attribute, does it compromise God's unity? The author argues that knowledge is not an external addition but a necessary concomitant of the essence's own perfection. Let's examine how this short argument dissolves the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the answer begins with 'والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية' — a direct response to a doubt.
The passage is concise, so pay attention to each phrase.
2
Identify the key premises
The passage puts forward two premises: (1) God's specific essence is perfect in itself, (2) from that perfection necessarily follows the attribute of knowledge. Locate these in the text.
The phrase 'من لوازم ذلك الكمال إيجابها لصفة العلم' expresses the second premise.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the doubt is removed ('فالشبهة زائلة'). Reflect on how acknowledging knowledge as a necessary concomitant—not an external addition—solves the problem.
This reasoning preserves both divine unity and the attribute of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن نقول : ذاته المخصوصة من حيث هي كاملة لعينها ولذاتها ، ومن لوازم ذلك الكمال إيجابها لصفة العلم (۱) وعلى هذا التقدير فالشبهة زائلة
The answer to the second doubt: We say: His specific essence, inasmuch as it is perfect in itself and for itself, and among the necessary concomitants of that perfection is that it necessitates the attribute of knowledge. On this supposition, the doubt is removed.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the second doubt, which presumably argued that God cannot have knowledge because it would imply multiplicity or dependence. The author responds by asserting that God's unique essence is perfect in and of itself. From this perfect essence, the attribute of knowledge necessarily follows as a necessary concomitant. Therefore, knowledge is not an external addition that compromises unity but is entailed by perfection itself. Thus the doubt vanishes.

Key Terms
ذات مخصوصة
كاملة لعينها ولذاتها
لازم الكمال
صفة العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribute of knowledge is an accidental addition to God's essence, implying multiplicity.
Correction: Knowledge is a necessary concomitant of the essence's perfection — not an external addition.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the second doubt, what is the basis for affirming the attribute of knowledge for God?
A) It is an accidental addition to the essence.
B) It is a necessary concomitant of the perfection of the essence.
C) It is acquired through external revelation only.
D) It is denied because it implies multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
God's perfect essence necessarily entails the attribute of knowledge, removing the doubt about multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is a necessary consequence of His perfect essence, then He knows all your faults without ever discovering them—He simply knows. Does that thought make you want to run from Him or run to Him? What sin are you currently nursing that you need to confess because He already sees it perfectly?
?
The author says God's knowledge is not an extra addition but flows from His perfection. Do you believe that God's knowledge of your struggles is part of His perfect love, or do you treat it as a threat? How would your day change if you acted as if His knowing you is a gift, not a risk?
?
This passage removes a doubt about multiplicity in God. What doubt in your own heart keeps you from fully trusting that God knows you completely and still loves you? Name that doubt, and commit one concrete step today to replace it with the truth from this passage.

Refuting the Third Doubt: Relational Nature of Causality
The author answers the third doubt by showing that denying knowledge leads to a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a self-defeating argument to defend divine knowledge against the challenge that it is a relation.

Core Idea
If denying knowledge because it's a relation is valid, then God cannot be the Creator—which is absurd.

The third doubt claims that God cannot have knowledge because knowledge is a relation dependent on an external object. The author responds by showing that this reasoning would equally deny God's role as the effecter of the world. Since the doubter presumably accepts that God is the Creator, the argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic sentence carefully, then compare with the literal translation. Note that the author is presenting a conditional argument.
The argument structure is: if X then Y, but Y is false, so X is false.
2
Identify the structure
The passage contains an 'if-then' argument: if the negation of knowledge is necessary because it is a relation, then it would also be necessary that God is not the effecter of the world. Recognize that this is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Apply the same logic
Understand that the doubter's reasoning is applied consistently: if relations are problematic for knowledge, they are also problematic for the attribute of being an effecter. Since the doubter accepts that God is the effecter, the doubt is invalid.
This exposes the contradiction in the doubter's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إنه إن لزم نفي العلم لكون العلم نسبة موقوفة على حصول المضاف الثاني ، لزم كونه مؤثراً في وجود العالم ، لأن المؤثر أيضاً من باب النسب والإضافات
The answer to the third doubt: If the negation of knowledge is necessary because knowledge is a relation dependent on the existence of a second relatum, then it would be necessary that He is an effecter in the existence of the world, because the effecter is also from the category of relations and additions.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage answers the third doubt, which likely argued that God cannot have knowledge because knowledge is a relation that depends on the existence of an external object, and such dependence is incompatible with divine simplicity. The author refutes this by showing the argument is self-defeating. If this reasoning is accepted, it would also lead to denying that God is an 'effecter' in the existence of the world, because being an effecter is also a relation. Since the doubter presumably accepts that God is the Creator, the argument is invalid.

Key Terms

نسبة
مضاف الثاني

نسب وإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects the relational nature of knowledge.
Correction: The author does not reject that knowledge is a relation; he shows that if relations are problematic, then being an effecter (which is also a relation) would be problematic, leading to a contradiction.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge is not a relation, so the doubt is invalid.
B) If denying knowledge because it is a relation is necessary, then God cannot be the effecter of the world, which is absurd.
C) The doubter is correct that knowledge is a relation but that doesn't affect unity.
D) The author does not answer the third doubt.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt is refuted by showing that its reasoning would also deny God's attribute of being the effecter of the world, which is a conclusion no Muslim accepts.

In your own spiritual life, are there areas where you accept one truth (e.g., God's mercy) but struggle with another (e.g., His justice)? Answering the Fourth Doubt: Unity Admits Many Relations
The author answers the fourth doubt by showing that unity admits many relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a mathematical analogy to show that essential unity is not compromised by having multiple relations.

Core Idea
Unity itself has infinite relations, so multiplicity does not destroy oneness.

In response to the fourth doubt that divine attributes would introduce multiplicity into God's essence, the author presents a powerful logical argument using the concept of unity itself. Even the number one, which is farthest from multiplicity, admits infinite relational properties. This short but profound passage establishes that essential unity can coexist with many relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the author states the answer, gives the analogy, and draws the conclusion.
The analogy is based on the number one.
2
Identify the analogy
The author says unity (the number one) is half of two, a third of three, a fourth of four, and so on infinitely. This shows that unity has many relations.
These relations are relational properties, not parts.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the multiplicity of additions and relations does not detract from essential unity. This directly refutes the doubt.
Essential unity remains intact despite external relations.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Think about how this analogy applies to God: having many attributes does not make His essence multiple, just as the number one has many relations yet remains one.
This is a key argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : إن أبعد الأشياء عن طبائع الكثرة : الوحدة ، ثم إنها نصف الاثنين ، وثلث الثلاثة ، وربع الأربعة ، وهلم جرا ، إلى ما لا نهاية له من النسب . فيثبت : أن كثرة الإضافات والنسب لا يقدح في حصول الوحدة الذاتية . فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب والله أعلم .
The answer to the fourth doubt: The farthest thing from the natures of multiplicity is unity, yet it is half of two, a third of three, a fourth of four, and so on, to an infinite number of relations. Thus it is established that the multiplicity of additions and relations does not detract from the attainment of essential unity. So this is the end of discourse on this chapter, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth doubt, which argued that affirming multiple attributes/relations would destroy divine unity. The author refutes this by a profound analogy. Unity is the farthest thing from multiplicity, yet the number one itself has infinite relational properties: it is half of two, a third of three, a fourth of four, infinitely. Despite these many relations, the number one remains essentially one. Similarly, God's essence remains one despite having multiple attributes or relations. The multiplicity of additions and relations does not compromise essential unity. The author then concludes with 'This is the end of discourse on this chapter, and God knows best.'

Key Terms

نسب
إضافات
وحدة ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has multiple attributes, His essence becomes multiple.
Correction: The example of unity shows that a single essence can have multiple relations without being multiple.

Checkpoint

A) The sun has many rays but is one.
B) Unity (the number one) has infinite relations like half of two, third of three, etc.
C) The soul has many faculties but is one.
D) A river has many tributaries but is one.

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity of relations does not compromise essential unity, as demonstrated by the number one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Clarifying the Philosophers' Position on God's Knowledge of Particulars
Al-Matalib critiques the report that philosophers deny God's knowledge of all particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the actual position of the philosophers regarding God's knowledge of particulars, as distinguished from the common oversimplified report.

Core Idea
Philosophers deny God's knowledge of changing and bodily particulars, not all particulars.

Many reports claim that philosophers deny God's knowledge of any particular. Al-Matalib challenges this oversimplification, explaining that philosophers actually affirm God's knowledge of essences and the first intellect, but deny knowledge of changing and bodily particulars in their specific measures. This lesson clarifies what is truly at stake in the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the author's critique of the common report.
Pay attention to the distinction between what philosophers affirm and deny.
2
Identify the common report
Find the sentence reporting that philosophers say God is not knowledgeable of particulars.
The author says this report is questionable (فيها نظر).
3
Understand the two affirmations
The author gives two reasons why philosophers cannot deny all particulars: God knows His own essence, and He knows the first intellect as its cause.
These show that philosophers affirm knowledge of some particulars.
4
Recognize the actual denial
The author clarifies that philosophers actually deny knowledge of changing things and bodily things in their specific measures.
This is the correct statement of their position.
5
Review the key terms
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic terms for particulars, change, and bodily things.
These terms are central to the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصلت السيادي
البحث عن كونه تعالى عالماً بالجزئيات
من الناس من يحكي عن الفلاسفة أنهم يقولون : إنه تعالى غـيـر عـالم بالجزئيات . وهذه الحكاية فيها نظر . وذلك لأن ذاته المخصوصة ذات معينة ، وهو عالم بتلك الذات المعينة . ولا معنى للجزيء إلا ذلك ، فيكون عالماً بالجزيء وأيضاً : إنه لذاته علة العقل الأول ، والظاهر من مذهبهم أنهم يعترفون بكونه تعالى عالماً به من حيث أنه هو ، بل الصحيح أن يقال : إنهم ينكرون كونه تعالى عالماً بالمتغيرات من حيث إنها متغيرة وينكرون كونه تعالى عالماً بالجسمانيات بحسب مقاديرها المعينة المخصوصة .
واحتجوا على إثبات مذهبهم بوجوه :
الحجة (1) الأولى : قالوا : لو فرضنا كونه عالماً بأن زيداً جالس في هذا المكان ، فإذا قام زيد من ذلك المكان (۲) فعلمه بكونه جالساً ، إن بقي كان ذلك جهلاً ، والجهل على الله تعالى محال . وإن لم يبق كان ذلك تغيراً ، والتغير على الله محال . فهذا هو الحرف الذي عليه تحويل القوم
واعلم ·
أن المتكلمين صاروا فريقين عند هذه الشبهة . فمنهم من يقول :
The third chapter? The discussion on His being knowledgeable of particulars. Among people are those who relate about the philosophers that they say: He is not knowledgeable of particulars. This report is questionable. That is because His specific essence is a determinate essence, and He is knowledgeable of that determinate essence. And there is no meaning of 'particular' except that, so He is knowledgeable of the particular. Also: He is by His essence the cause of the first intellect, and the apparent from their doctrine is that they acknowledge that He is knowledgeable of it insofar as it is He, rather the correct thing to say is that they deny that He is knowledgeable of changing things insofar as they are changing, and they deny that He is knowledgeable of bodily things according to their specific determined measures. And they argued for establishing their doctrine with proofs: The first proof (1): They said: If we suppose that He is knowledgeable that Zayd is sitting in this place, then if Zayd leaves that place (2), His knowledge of his being sitting—if it remains, that would be ignorance, and ignorance of God is impossible. And if it does not remain, that would be change, and change of God is impossible. So this is the point upon which the people's debate revolves. And know that the theologians became two groups regarding this doubt. Some of them say:
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on God's knowledge of particulars, a key issue in Islamic theology. The author begins by noting that some people report that philosophers claim God is not knowledgeable of particulars, but the author finds this report questionable. He explains that God's specific essence is itself a determinate entity, and God knows that essence, so He knows at least that particular. Furthermore, as the cause of the first intellect, philosophers would acknowledge God's knowledge of it. However, the author clarifies that what philosophers actually deny is God's knowledge of changing things insofar as they change, and of bodily things in their specific quantitative measures. The author then presents the first proof used by philosophers to support their denial. The proof is: if God knew that Zayd is sitting in a place, and then Zayd leaves, God's knowledge of Zayd's sitting either remains or does not remain. If it remains while Zayd is no longer sitting, that would be ignorance—impossible for God. If it does not remain, that implies change in God—also impossible. Therefore, God cannot know changing particulars. The author then notes that theologians split into two groups in response to this doubt, but the passage ends before detailing their positions.

Key Terms
الجزئيات (particulars)
الجزيء (the particular)
المتغيرات (changing things)
الجسمانيات (bodily things)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That philosophers deny God knows any particular at all.
Correction: Philosophers affirm God's knowledge of essences and the first intellect, but deny knowledge of changing and bodily particulars in their specific measures.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Matalib's clarification, what do philosophers actually deny about God's knowledge?
A) God knows no particulars at all.
B) God knows everything, including changing things.
C) God knows changing things and bodily things in specific measures.
D) God does not know His own essence.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers deny God's knowledge of changing and bodily particulars, not of essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that some parts of your life—your daily habits, your physical needs, your changing emotions—are too small or insignificant for God to notice? The First Proof: The Dilemma of Change and Ignorance
Al-Matalib presents the philosophers' first proof against God knowing changing particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first proof that God cannot know changing particulars, based on the impossibility of ignorance and change in God.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of a changing particular would either become ignorance or require change in God — both impossible.

The philosophers present a powerful argument: if God knew Zayd was sitting, and then Zayd stands, the knowledge either remains (becoming ignorance) or changes (implying change in God). Both are impossible, so God cannot know such changing particulars. This lesson unpacks the logic of this dilemma and notes that theologians responded by splitting into two groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the philosophers' first proof, focusing on the scenario with Zayd.
Note that the passage continues from the previous section.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The proof says that after Zayd stands, God's knowledge of him sitting either remains or does not remain. Understand each possibility.
These two options exhaust the logical possibilities.
3
Analyze why each horn is impossible
If the knowledge remains, it is ignorance (since reality changed). If it does not remain, that implies change in God. Both are impossible for God.
Ignorance is a deficiency; change is also impossible for the unchanging God.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Therefore, God cannot know changing particulars. This is the core of the philosophers' argument.
The passage calls this 'the point upon which the people's debate revolves'.
5
Note the theologians' response
The passage ends by noting that theologians split into two groups regarding this doubt, but does not elaborate here.
This sets up future discussion.

Source Text

Correcting the Attribution to Philosophers on God's Knowledge of Particulars
Corrects the view attributed to philosophers regarding God's knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophers did not deny all knowledge of particulars, but only knowledge of changing and material particulars in specific measures.

Core Idea
The philosophers only denied God's knowledge of changing and material particulars, not all particulars.

Many students of Islamic philosophy have been taught that the philosophers denied God's knowledge of particulars entirely. However, this is a simplified and inaccurate account. In this passage, the author clarifies the philosophers' actual position, showing that they did affirm knowledge of some particulars, such as God's own essence and the First Intellect. We will read the source text and unpack its nuanced correction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the author's initial statement and then the reasoning.
The author begins by stating the topic and then critiques the common report.
2
Identify the two arguments
Look for two reasons the author gives that the philosophers cannot deny all particular knowledge.
First reason: God knows His own essence which is particular. Second: He knows the First Intellect.
3
Understand the correct position
The author then states what the philosophers actually deny: knowledge of changing things and material bodies in specific measures.
This is the key correction.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this nuance matters for understanding the philosophers' theology.
It shows they did not deny all particular knowledge, only specific types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث عن كونه تعالى عالماً بالجزئيات. من الناس من يحكي عن الفلاسفة أنهم يقولون : إنه تعالى غير عالم بالجزئيات. وهذه الحكاية فيها نظر. وذلك لأن ذاته المخصوصة ذات معينة، وهو عالم بتلك الذات المعينة. ولا معنى للجزيء إلا ذلك، فيكون عالماً بالجزيء. وأيضاً: إنه لذاته علة العقل الأول، والظاهر من مذهبهم أنهم يعترفون بكونه تعالى عالماً به من حيث أنه هو، بل الصحيح أن يقال: إنهم ينكرون كونه تعالى عالماً بالمتغيرات من حيث إنها متغيرة، وينكرون كونه تعالى عالماً بالجسمانيات بحسب مقاديرها المعينة المخصوصة.
Investigation of His being knowledgeable of particulars. Among people are those who relate from the philosophers that they say: He, exalted, is not knowledgeable of particulars. This narration [requires] scrutiny. That is because His specific essence is a particular essence, and He is knowledgeable of that specific essence. And there is no meaning of 'particular' except that; thus He is knowledgeable of the particular. Also: He is, by His essence, the cause of the First Intellect, and the apparent from their doctrine is that they acknowledge that He, exalted, is knowledgeable of it inasmuch as it is He. Rather the correct thing to say is that they deny that He, exalted, is knowledgeable of changing things inasmuch as they are changing, and they deny that He, exalted, is knowledgeable of material bodies according to their specific particular measures.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the topic: the investigation of God's knowledge of particulars. It then clarifies a common misattribution to the philosophers. The author notes that some people claim that the philosophers say God does not know particulars at all, but this claim requires scrutiny. He gives two reasons: first, God's own essence is a particular, and He knows it; second, the philosophers hold that God is the cause of the First Intellect and they acknowledge He knows it. The correct understanding is that the philosophers deny knowledge of changing particulars (such as temporal events) and of physical bodies in their specific quantities. Thus, the passage provides a nuanced correction to the simplistic attribution.

Misconception: Philosophers deny God's knowledge of all particulars.
Correction: They only deny knowledge of changing particulars and material bodies in specific measures.

B) They deny knowledge of changing particulars and material bodies in specific measures.
C) They affirm knowledge of everything including particulars.

Key Takeaway

The Dilemma of Divine Knowledge of Changing Particulars
The philosophers' first argument: a dilemma about knowledge of changing particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous philosophical argument that God cannot know changing particulars, and the logical dilemma it presents.

Core Idea
For God to know a changing particular would require either ignorance or change — both impossible.

This passage presents the first and most famous argument used by philosophers to deny that God has knowledge of changing particulars. The argument is a classic logical dilemma: if God knows something that later changes, either His knowledge stays the same (becoming false) or it changes (implying change in God). Both options are deemed impossible. Understanding this dilemma is crucial for engaging with the Islamic theological response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the dilemma become clear.
Notice the concise phrasing of the two alternatives.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two options after Zaid stands: either God's knowledge remains, or it does not remain. Both lead to an impossibility.
3
Understand why each option is impossible
If the knowledge remains, it becomes ignorance (since the fact changed). If it does not remain, that implies change in God—both are impossible for God.
Ignorance is a deficiency; change is incompatible with divine perfection.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that God cannot know changing particulars. Ask yourself whether this conclusion necessarily follows from the dilemma, and what assumptions it makes about the nature of knowledge.
This sets the stage for the theological response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا على إثبات مذهبهم بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: قالوا: لو فرضنا كونه عالماً بأن زيداً جالس في هذا المكان، فإذا قام زيد من ذلك المكان فعلمه بكونه جالساً، إن بقي كان ذلك جهلاً، والجهل على الله تعالى محال. وإن لم يبق كان ذلك تغيراً، والتغير على الله محال. فهذا هو الحرف الذي عليه تحويل القوم.
And they argued for establishing their doctrine with proofs: The first argument: They said: If we suppose that He is knowledgeable that Zaid is sitting in this place, then when Zaid stands from that place, His knowledge of his being sitting: if it remains, that would be ignorance, and ignorance of God, exalted, is impossible. And if it does not remain, that would be change, and change regarding God is impossible. So this is the point on which the people's discourse turns.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the philosophers, which is a classic dilemma. If God knows that Zaid is sitting, then when Zaid stands up, one of two things must happen to God's knowledge: either the knowledge that Zaid is sitting remains, which would be ignorance since the fact has changed, or it ceases, which would be a change in God. Both possibilities are deemed impossible for God. Therefore, the philosophers conclude that God cannot know changing particulars. This argument is the core of their position and is often referred to as the 'shubha' or sophism.

Key Terms
حجة
جزئي
علم
جهل
تغير
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that God cannot know any particular things at all.
Correction: The argument specifically targets changing particulars (those that undergo change over time). It does not address God's knowledge of unchanging particulars or universal knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma presented in the philosophers' argument?
A) Either God's knowledge is eternal or created.
B) Either God's knowledge remains (leading to ignorance) or changes (impossible).
C) Either God's knowledge is general or specific.
D) Either God knows all or He knows nothing.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first argument uses a dilemma to show that God's knowledge of changing particulars would necessitate either ignorance or change in God, both of which are impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you worry that God's knowledge of you changes—that He sees you differently now? This lesson shows that change in God is impossible. How does this truth affect your view of sin and repentance? Do you believe that God's knowledge of your standing before Him never changes, even when you change?

The philosophers use a logical dilemma to argue that God cannot know changing particulars. But have you ever prioritized logical neatness over trusting God's revealed attributes? In what ways does this lesson push you to accept that God's knowledge is beyond full human comprehension, and how can you cultivate trust in His omniscience even when it seems paradoxical?

Two Responses and Four Proofs on the Permanence of God's Knowledge
Describes two responses to the philosophers' argument and presents four proofs from one group.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the split among mutakallimun regarding God's knowledge and the four proofs offered by those who held that knowledge remains unchanged.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not change; only the known objects change.

The philosophers' argument that God's knowledge must change when future events occur prompted two responses among the mutakallimun. One group insisted that knowledge itself remains the same, while the other accepted change. The first group offered four proofs to support their view, drawing on the oneness of God's knowledge, an analogy with a mirror, an example of human belief, and the nature of knowledge as an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two positions
Read the opening lines to see how the mutakallimun split into two groups: those who say knowledge remains and those who say it changes.
Note that both groups are responding to the same philosophical challenge.
2
Examine the first two proofs
Look at the first proof from the oneness of knowledge and the second proof using the mirror analogy. Notice how they argue that multiplicity or change in objects does not affect the knower's attribute.
3
Reflect on the third and fourth proofs
Consider the third proof using human belief and the fourth proof about knowledge as a real attribute. Ask yourself how these reinforce the idea that knowledge itself remains unchanged.
The fourth proof is incomplete in the text.
4
Summarize the arguments
Try to restate each proof in your own words. This will help solidify the reasoning of the first group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المتكلمين صاروا فريقين عند هذه الشبهة. فمنهم من يقول: العلم الأول باقي. ومنهم من التزم التغير. وقال: العلم الأول غير باقي. أما الفريق الأول فقالوا: إن العلم بأن الشيء سيحدث هو نفس العلم بحدوثه إذا حدث، فالعلم باقي والتغير واقع في المعلوم. واحتجوا على صحة قولهم بوجوه: الأول: إنه تعالى يعلم بالعلم الواحد جميع المعلومات. فلما لم يلزم من تكثر تلك المعلومات تكثر العلم. وجب أن لا يلزم أيضاً من تغير المعلومات تغير العلم. والثاني: إن العلم صفة ينكشف بها المعلومات كما هي. ونذكر لهذا المعنى مثالاً. فنقول: إنا إذا فرضنا مرآة منصوبة على الجدار، فكل من مرّ بتلك المرآة انكشفت صورته فيها. فعند مرور الناس على تلك المرآة لا يزال ينكشف فيها صورة بعد صورة إلا أن المرآة لم تتغير عن حالها. بل التغير إنما وقع في الناس الذين يمرون عليها. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: العلم صفة متهيئة الإدراك ما يعرض عليها، فتلك الصفة باقية بحالها، والتغير واقع في المعلومات. الوجه الثالث: قالوا: إذا فرضنا أن إنساناً اعتقد أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً، وفرضنا ذلك الاعتقاد إلى أن دخل زيد البلد، فإن تعين ذلك، يصير معتقداً لكون زيد داخلاً في البلد في هذه الساعة. فيثبت بما ذكرنا أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. والرابع: هو أن العلم صفة حقيقية قائمة بذات العالم وتعلقه بالمعلوم.
And know that the mutakallimun (theologians) became two parties regarding this sophism. Among them those who say: The first knowledge remains. And among them those who committed to change, and said: The first knowledge is not remaining. As for the first party, they said: Knowledge of a thing that it will occur is the same knowledge of its occurrence when it occurs, so the knowledge remains and change occurs in the known. And they argued for the correctness of their statement with proofs: The first: He, exalted, knows with one knowledge all known things. Since multiplicity of those known things did not necessitate multiplicity of knowledge, it must also not necessitate change of knowledge due to change of known things. The second: Knowledge is an attribute by which known things are disclosed as they are. And we mention an example for this meaning. We say: If we suppose a mirror set up on the wall, then every person who passes by that mirror, his image is disclosed in it. At the passing of people by that mirror, there continues to be disclosed image after image except that the mirror does not change from its state. Rather change only occurred in the people who pass by it. If you have understood this, we say: Knowledge is an attribute disposed to perceive what occurs to it, so that attribute remains in its state, and change occurs in the known things. The third: They said: If we suppose that a person believes that Zaid will enter the house tomorrow, and we suppose that belief until Zaid entered the town, then it becomes specified, he becomes a believer of Zaid being inside the town at this hour. So it is established by what we have mentioned that knowledge of a thing that it will exist is the same knowledge of its existence when it exists. And the fourth: Knowledge is a real attribute subsisting in the essence of the knower, and its relation to the known.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage begins by noting that the mutakallimun split into two groups when confronted with the philosophers' sophism. One group says God's original knowledge of a future event remains unchanged even after the event occurs; the other group accepts that knowledge changes. The first group then argues that knowledge of a thing's future occurrence is identical to knowledge of its present occurrence. They provide four proofs. The first proof: God knows all things with one knowledge, so multiplicity of objects does not multiply knowledge, and by the same logic, change of objects does not necessitate change of knowledge. The second proof uses an analogy of a mirror: images change but the mirror remains the same; similarly, God's knowledge is an attribute that remains unchanged while the known objects change. The third proof uses an example of human belief: if someone believes Zaid will enter tomorrow, that same belief becomes the belief that he is entering now when the event occurs. The fourth proof (incomplete) states that knowledge is a real attribute subsisting in the knower, implying that change in the known does not affect the attribute itself. The enumeration is complete within the available text.

Key Terms

شبهة
علم
تغير
معلوم
صفة
مرآة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the first group denies any change in knowledge altogether.
Correction: They affirm that knowledge itself remains unchanged, but the relationship (or the known) changes. The knowledge remains identical while the object changes.

Checkpoint
Which proof uses the illustration of a mirror to argue that God's knowledge remains unchanged?
A) The first proof.
B) The second proof.
C) The third proof.
D) The fourth proof.

Key Takeaway
The first group of mutakallimun argued that God's knowledge remains unchanged because knowledge is not affected by changes in the known objects, as demonstrated by four proofs.

The First Argument: Unity of Divine Knowledge
First argument for the immutability of the first knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's single knowledge of all things implies that change in known objects does not require change in knowledge itself.

Core Idea
God's one knowledge encompasses all change without itself changing.

The first group argues that knowledge of a future event is identical to knowledge when it occurs. Their first proof draws on the unity of divine knowledge: if many known objects do not multiply God's knowledge, then changing objects should not change it. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first argument
Read the Arabic and translation of the opening statement and the first argument. Notice the logical structure: premise, analogy, conclusion.
The argument uses an a fortiori reasoning from multiplicity to change.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Find the premise that God knows all with one knowledge, then the implication that change in objects does not necessitate change in knowledge.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider how the argument compares multiplicity of objects to change of objects. Why would the same reasoning apply?
Think about the principle: if X does not affect Y, then a stronger case of X should also not affect Y.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفريق الأول فقالوا: إن العلم بأن الشيء سيحدث هو نفس العلم بحدوثه إذا حدث، فالعلم باق والتغير واقع في المعلوم. واحتجوا على صحة قولهم بوجوه: الأول: إنه تعالى يعلم بالعلم الواحد جميع المعلومات، فلما لم يلزم من تكثر تلك المعلومات تكثر العلم، وجب أن لا يلزم أيضاً من تغير المعلومات تغير العلم. والثاني: إن العلم صفة ينكشف بها المعلومات كما هي. ونذكر لهذا المعنى مثالاً فنقول: إنا إذا فرضنا مرآة منصوبة على الجدار، فكل من مر بتلك المرآة انكشفت صورته فيها، فعند مرور الناس على تلك المرآة لا يزال ينكشف فيها صورة بعد صورة، إلا أن المرآة لم تتغير عن حالها، بل التغير إنما وقع في الناس الذين يمرون عليها. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: العلم صفة متهيئة لإدراك ما يعرض عليها، فتلك الصفة باقية بحالها، والتغير واقع في المعلومات. الوجه الثالث: قالوا: إذا فرضنا أن إنساناً اعتقد أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً، وفرضنا ذلك الاعتقاد إلى أن دخل زيد البلد، فإن تعين ذلك، يصير معتقداً لكون زيد داخلاً في البلد في هذه الساعة. فيثبت بما ذكرنا أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. والرابع: هو أن العلم صفة حقيقية قائمة بذات العالم وتعلقه بالمعلوم نسبة بين العلم وبين المعلوم، فإذا تغير المعلوم فقد تغيرت نسبة ذلك العلم إلى المعلوم، ولم يتغير ذات العلم. ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا كان جالساً في مكان نفسه فجاء إنسان وجلس على أحد جانبيه، كان هذا الجالس يميناً له، فإذا قام ذلك الإنسان، وانتقل من ذلك الجانب إلى الجانب الآخر صار الإنسان الأول يساراً له، بعد أن كان يميناً له، فههنا وقع التغير والتبدل في هذه الإضافة، مع صريح الحس والعقل يدل على أنه لم يقع فيه التغير البتة، بل هو باقي كما كان. فهذا جملة الوجوه التي يتمسك بها من يقول العلم الأول باقي كما كان.
As for the first group, they said: Indeed, the knowledge that a thing will happen is the very knowledge of its happening when it happens, so the knowledge remains and the change occurs in the known object. They argued for the correctness of their statement with aspects: First: He, exalted, knows all known things with one knowledge. Since the multiplicity of those known things does not necessitate multiplicity of knowledge, it must be that the change of the known things also does not necessitate change of knowledge. Second: Knowledge is an attribute by which known things are disclosed as they are. We mention for this meaning an example, so we say: If we suppose a mirror set up on a wall, then every person who passes by that mirror, his image is disclosed in it. So at the passing of people by that mirror, image after image continues to be disclosed in it, except that the mirror has not changed from its state. Rather, the change only occurred in the people who pass by it. If you understand this, we say: Knowledge is an attribute prepared to perceive what is presented to it, so that attribute remains in its state, and change occurs in the known things. The third aspect: They said: If we suppose that a person believes that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, and we suppose that belief until Zayd entered the town, then when that is determined, he becomes a believer of Zayd being inside the town at this hour. So it is established by what we mentioned that the knowledge that a thing will exist is the very knowledge of its existence when it exists. The fourth: Knowledge is a real attribute subsisting in the essence of the knower, and its relation to the known is a relation between knowledge and the known. So when the known changes, the relation of that knowledge to the known has changed, and the essence of knowledge has not changed. Do you not see that if a man is sitting in one place, then a person comes and sits on one of his sides, this sitter is to his right; then if that person stands and moves from that side to the other side, the first person becomes to his left, after he was to his right? So here change and alteration occurred in this addition, while clear sense and reason indicate that no change occurred in him at all; rather, he remains as he was. This is the sum of the aspects held by those who say the first knowledge remains as it was.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments (wujuh) from the group who hold that the first knowledge (al-ilm al-awwal) of a future event remains unchanged when the event occurs. The author first states their position: the knowledge that something will happen is identical to the knowledge of its happening when it happens; change occurs only in the known object, not in the knowledge itself. Then he lists their four proofs. The first argument appeals to divine knowledge: God knows all things with one single knowledge; multiplicity of objects does not multiplicity of knowledge, so change of objects should not necessitate change of knowledge. The second argument uses the analogy of a mirror: a mirror reflects successive images without itself changing; similarly, knowledge is a stable attribute that receives changing known objects without alteration. The third argument considers a person's belief that Zayd will enter tomorrow; when Zayd actually enters, the same belief becomes the knowledge of his present entrance—showing continuity. The fourth argument explains that knowledge is a real attribute with a relation to the known; change in the known only changes the relation, not the essence of knowledge, just as a person's left-right orientation changes while the person remains unchanged. The passage concludes by summarizing these as the arguments of the group that asserts the first knowledge remains as it was.

المعلوم
التغير

Key Takeaway
The unity of divine knowledge shows that it remains unchanged despite multiplicity or change in objects.
