The Will's Influence as Preference and the Objection of Regress
Author explains the will's influence as preference, not necessity, and answers an objection about regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine will influences by way of preference (tarjih), not necessity or creation, and why the objection of infinite regress is invalid.

Core Idea
The will's influence is by preference, not compulsion – a key to divine free choice.

In this passage, the author explains that the will's influence in specifying possibilities is a matter of preponderance (tarjih), not necessity or creation. He then addresses an objection that any influence without compulsion is rationally impossible. Through a careful analysis of two scenarios, he shows that the objection either leads to infinite regress or undermines the very concept of free will, thus preserving the doctrine of God as a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the compressed style; the argument is dense.
2
Identify the author's claim
Find where the author states that the will's influence is by way of preference, not by way of necessity or creation.
This is the core thesis.
3
Understand the objection
The objector claims that influence without compulsion or creation is impossible. See how the author sets it up.
The objection is a challenge to the very possibility of free choice.
4
Follow the two-case analysis
The author considers two possibilities: either the will can preponderate at another time or it is fixed to one time. See the consequences of each.
This is the heart of the refutation.
5
Note the final argument
The author concludes that if the will can be specified to one aspect, then power would not need the will, which is unacceptable.
This ties back to preserving God as a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تأثيرها في التخصيص إن كان على سبيل الوجوب كان المؤثر موجباً بالذات، وإن كان على سبيل الصفة فهو المطلوب، لأن هذه الصفة صفة تقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا على سبيل الإيجاب، ولا على سبيل التكوين. وذلك هو المطلوب. وهذا تقرير هذا الدليل على أحسن الوجوه. ولقائل أن يقول: قد بينا أن المؤثر الذي يؤثر لا على سبيل الإيجاب ولا على سبيل التكوين محال في العقول، فلا فائدة في الإعادة. فما ذكرناه برهان قاطع في مقابلة ما ذكرتم. ثم نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر في ذلك التخصيص هو القدرة؟ قوله: لأن القدرة نسبتها إلى الإيجاد في جميع الأوقات على السوية. قلنا: إما أن نقول: الإرادة صالحة لترجيح هذا الفعل بوقت آخر، كما أنها صالحة لترجيحه بهذا الوقت. وإما أن يقولوا: إنها صالحة لتخصيص العالم بذلك الوقت، مع أنه يمتنع تعلقها بتخصيص العالم بوقت آخر. فإن كان الأول فنقول: صلاحية الصفة للوجوه الكثيرة إما أن تحوج إلى المرجح أو لا تحوج، فإن أحوجته إليه لزم افتقار الإرادة إلى مرجح آخر، وإن لم تحوجه إليه وجب أن لا تحتاج القدرة إلى الإرادة. وأما القسم الثاني: فنقول: لما كانت الإرادة أزلية ممتنعة العدم، وكانت متعلقة بأحداث العالم في وقت معين، وما كانت صالحة للتعلق بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر، فحينئذ يصير الإله موجباً بالذات غير فاعل على سبيل الصحة. ومعلوم أن هذه المسألة فرع على إثبات القادر المختار، وكل فرع يوجب فساد الأصل كان باطلاً. وأيضاً: فإذا جاز أن يقال: تلك الإرادة المخصوصة متعينة التعلق بذلك الوجه الواحد، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال تلك الإرادة المخصوصة متعينة التعلق بوجه واحد، وحينئذ تكون القدرة غنية عن الإرادة المرجحة؟ وهذا تمام البحث في هذا الدليل.
Then its influence in specification: if it is by way of necessity, the agent would be an agent by essence; but if it is by way of attribute, then it is what is sought. Because this attribute is an attribute that necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other, not by way of compulsion, nor by way of creation. That is what is sought. And this is the exposition of this proof in the best manner. And an objector may say: We have already shown that an agent that influences neither by way of compulsion nor by way of creation is impossible in the intellect, so there is no benefit in repetition. So what we have mentioned is a decisive proof in opposition to what you mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the agent in that specification be power? He says: Because the relation of power to bringing into existence in all times is uniform. We say: Either we say: the will is capable of preponderating this act at another time, just as it is capable of preponderating it at this time. Or they say: It is capable of specifying the world at that time, while it is impossible for it to be attached to specifying the world at another time. If it is the first, then we say: The capacity of the attribute for many aspects either necessitates a preponderator or does not. If it necessitates it, then the will would require another preponderator; and if it does not necessitate it, then it would be necessary that power does not need the will. As for the second division, we say: Since the will is eternal, impossible of non-existence, and is attached to the events of the world at a specific time, and it is not capable of being attached to bringing about the world at another time, then in that case the deity becomes an agent by essence, not an agent in the valid sense. And it is known that this issue is a branch of establishing the free agent, and any branch that entails the corruption of the root is invalid. Also: If it is allowed to say: that particular will is specified for attachment to that one aspect, then why is it not allowed to say: that particular will is specified for attachment to one aspect, and then power would be independent of the preponderating will? This is the conclusion of the discussion in this proof.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the mode of influence of the divine will (irada). The author first clarifies that the attribute's influence is by way of preference (tarjih), not by necessity (wujub) or creation (takwin). This is what they aim to prove. Then an objection is raised: the opponent claims that an influence that is neither by compulsion nor creation is rationally impossible. The author responds by examining two possible cases. The first case: if the will can favor an act at another time as well, then the will's capacity for many aspects either requires a separate preponderator (leading to infinite regress) or does not—if not, then power would not need will at all. The second case: if the will is necessarily tied to one specific time, then God becomes a necessary agent, not a free chooser, which undermines the very premise of free will. The author argues that this line of reasoning would corrupt the foundational belief in God's free choice. Finally, they point out that if the opponent allows the will to be specified to one aspect, they should also allow power to be independent of will. Thus the argument holds.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التخصيص
الترجيح
الوجوب
التكوين
القدرة
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any influence that is not by compulsion or creation is rationally impossible.
Correction: Influence by preference (tarjīḥ) is possible and necessary to avoid determinism, as the author demonstrates by showing the consequences of the objection.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the objection that an influence without compulsion or creation is impossible?
A) He accepts it and redefines the will.
B) He argues that the will's influence is still possible through preference and refutes the objection by analyzing its consequences.
C) He says the objection is irrelevant.
D) He concedes that the proof fails.

Key Takeaway
The divine will influences by preference, and the objection of impossibility is refuted by showing it either leads to regress or denies free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector thought that any influence not by compulsion or creation is impossible. In your life, have you ever assumed that God must act in a certain way based on your own logic? How does this lesson call you to surrender to the reality of God's free choice, even when it defies human reasoning?
?
The author argues that if the will could only attach to one time, God would become a necessary agent. Do you treat God as a necessary cause—expecting specific outcomes based on your actions—or as a free agent who chooses? What would change in your worship if you truly believed in God's unrestrained free will?
?
The dilemma in this lesson exposes the fallacy of an infinite regress of reasons. Consider your own choices: do you act as though your decisions are determined by an endless chain of causes, or do you recognize a moment of genuine preference? How does affirming God's free will by preference challenge you to exercise your own free will in obedience to Him?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Three Arguments of the Deniers of Divine Will
Author presents three arguments against divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main philosophical arguments used by those who deny the divine attribute of will, as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Opponents of divine will argue it leads to necessity, implies human traits, or forces a false dilemma.

In this passage, the author presents three distinct arguments (hujaj) that critics of the divine attribute of will have historically used. These arguments challenge the coherence of affirming will for God by pointing out logical consequences and conceptual limitations. As you read, note how each argument targets a different aspect of the concept of will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on the structure: the author enumerates three proofs with connecting phrases.
2
Identify the first argument (perfection dilemma)
The first argument uses a conditional: if will is perfect, act is necessary; if imperfect, act is impossible. Understand why both horns are problematic.
3
Identify the second argument (conceptual limitation)
The second argument claims we only know will as inclination to benefit or avoid harm, which cannot apply to God. Any other meaning is unintelligible.
4
Identify the third argument (eternal/temporal dichotomy)
The third argument presents a dichotomy: will must be either eternal or created. Both options are deemed impossible. Think about why each is rejected.
5
Reflect on the author's stance
Note that the author does not refute these arguments here; he simply presents them. This sets up the next discussion. Consider what a response might look like.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة: واحتج المنكرون لهذه الصفة بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: إنها إن كانت تامة في جميع جهات المؤثرية وجب الفعل، وإن كانت غير تامة امتنع الفعل، فالقول بإثبات هذه الإرادة محال. الحجة الثانية: إنا لا نعقل من الإرادة إلا الميل إلى جلب النفع أو إلى دفع الضرر، وذلك في حق الله محال. وأما الإرادة بمعنى آخر فلا نعقله البتة، فكان القول بإثباته لله محالاً. الحجة الثالثة: لو حصلت الإرادة لكانت إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة، والقسمان باطلان، فالقول بإثبات الإرادة باطل. وهو معلوم.
The third issue: And the deniers of this attribute argued with proofs: The first argument: If it (the will) is complete in all aspects of effective agency, the act is necessary; and if it is incomplete, the act is impossible. So the claim to affirm this will is impossible. The second argument: We do not conceive of will except as inclination to bring benefit or repel harm, and that is impossible with respect to God. As for will in another sense, we do not conceive it at all. So the claim to affirm it for God is impossible. The third argument: If will existed, it would either be eternal or temporal, and both divisions are false. So the claim to affirm will is false. And it is known.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (hujaj) used by those who deny the attribute of divine will (irada). The first argument is a dilemma: if the will is perfect in every aspect of causality, then the act must necessarily follow, making it compulsory rather than free; if it is imperfect, the act cannot occur at all. Either way, affirming will is problematic. The second argument is that human understanding of will is only as a desire for benefit or avoidance of harm, both of which are impossible for God. Any other meaning of will is unintelligible. The third argument is a temporal/eternal dichotomy: the will must be either eternal or created; both are deemed impossible: an eternal will would necessitate the eternal existence of its object, and a created will would imply change in God. The author ends by noting that these arguments are well-known, but he does not refute them in this passage (likely awaiting subsequent discussion). This lesson simply records the objections of the opponents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments against divine will are conclusive and irrefutable.
Correction: The author presents them as objections to be refuted later, not as final conclusions.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against divine will presented in this passage?
A) Will requires a purpose, which is impossible for God.
B) If will is perfect, the act is necessary; if imperfect, the act is impossible.
C) Will cannot be both eternal and created.
D) Will is unknown to humans.

Key Takeaway
The deniers of divine will present three distinct arguments: the perfection dilemma, the conceptual limitation, and the eternal/temporal dichotomy, all of which must be addressed in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though God's will is perfectly effective in every detail of your life, or do you find yourself acting as if some things are outside His control? What would change if you fully internalized that His will is neither deficient nor compelled?
?
When you face a trial, do you catch yourself assuming that God would only will something beneficial or harmful based on your human perspective? How can you remind yourself that His will is beyond your conception of benefit and harm, yet always wise?
?
The third argument presents a false choice: God's will must be either eternal or temporal. In your dua, do you sometimes pray as if God's will is temporal and subject to change? How can you affirm His eternal will while still pleading for His mercy?
